This allows us to interact with the Facade directly rather than the subsystem behind the scenes. Whenever we use jQuery's <code>$(el).css()</code> or <code>$(el).animate()</code> methods, we're actually using a Facade - the simpler public interface that avoids us having to manually call the many internal methods in jQuery core required to get some behavior working. This also avoids the need to manually interact with DOM APIs and maintain state variables.</p>

<p>The jQuery core methods should be considered intermediate abstractions. The more immediate burden to developers is the DOM API and facades are what make the jQuery library so easy to use.</p>

<p>
To build on what we've learned, the Facade pattern both simplifies the interface of a class and it also decouples the class from the code that utilizes it. This gives us the ability to indirectly interact with subsystems in a way that can sometimes be less prone to error than accessing the subsystem directly. A Facade's advantages include ease of use and often a small size-footprint in implementing the pattern. </p>

<p>
Let’s take a look at the pattern in action. This is an unoptimized code example, but here we're utilizing a Facade to simplify an interface for listening to events cross-browser. We do this by creating a common method that can be used in one’s code which does the task of checking for the existence of features so that it can provide a safe and cross-browser compatible solution.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_241977"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>addMyEvent = </code><code>function</code><code>( el,ev,fn ){</code></div><div><code>if</code><code>( el.addEventListener ){</code></div><div><code>el.addEventListener( ev,fn, </code><code>false</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>}</code><code>else</code> <code>if</code><code>(el.attachEvent){</code></div><div><code>el.attachEvent( </code><code>"on"</code> <code>+ ev, fn );</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>else</code><code>{</code></div><div><code>el[</code><code>"on"</code> <code>+ ev] = fn;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>
In a similar manner, we're all familiar with jQuery's <code>$(document).ready(..)</code>. Internally, this is actually being powered by a method called <code>bindReady()</code>, which is doing this:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_453122"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>bindReady: </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>...</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( document.addEventListener ) {</code></div><div><code>// Use the handy event callback</code></div><div><code>document.addEventListener( </code><code>"DOMContentLoaded"</code><code>, DOMContentLoaded, </code><code>false</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// A fallback to window.onload, that will always work</code></div><div><code>window.addEventListener( </code><code>"load"</code><code>, jQuery.ready, </code><code>false</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// If IE event model is used</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>else</code> <code>if</code> <code>( document.attachEvent ) {</code></div><div><code>document.attachEvent( </code><code>"onreadystatechange"</code><code>, DOMContentLoaded );</code></div><div><code>// A fallback to window.onload, that will always work</code></div><div><code>window.attachEvent( </code><code>"onload"</code><code>, jQuery.ready );</code></div><div><code>...</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>
This is another example of a Facade, where the rest of the world simply uses the limited interface exposed by <code>$(document).ready(..)</code> and the more complex implementation powering it is kept hidden from sight.</p>

<p>
Facades don't just have to be used on their own, however. They can also be integrated with other patterns such as the Module pattern. As we can see below, our instance of the module patterns contains a number of methods which have been privately defined. A Facade is then used to supply a much simpler API to accessing these methods:
</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_993518"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>module = (</code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>_private = {</code></div><div><code>i: 5,</code></div><div><code>get: </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"current value:"</code> <code>+ </code><code>this</code><code>.i);</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>set: </code><code>function</code><code>( val ) {</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.i = val;</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>run: </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"running"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>jump: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"jumping"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>facade: </code><code>function</code><code>( args ) {</code></div><div><code>_private.set(args.val);</code></div><div><code>_private.get();</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( args.run ) {</code></div><div><code>_private.run();</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>}());</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: "current value: 10" and "running"</code></div><div><code>module.facade( {run: </code><code>true</code><code>, val: 10} );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>
In this example, calling <code>module.facade()</code> will actually trigger a set of private behavior within the module, but again, the user isn't concerned with this. we've made it much easier for them to consume a feature without needing to worry about implementation-level details.
</p>

<h3>Notes on abstraction</h3>

<p>Facades generally have few disadvantages, but one concern worth noting is performance. Namely, one must determine whether there is an implicit cost to the abstraction a Facade offers to our implementation and if so, whether this cost is justifiable. Going back to the jQuery library, most of us are aware that both <code>getElementById("identifier")</code> and <code>$("#identifier")</code> can be used to query an element on a page by its ID.</p>

<p>Did you know however that <code>getElementById()</code> on its own is significantly faster by a high order of magnitude? Take a look at this jsPerf test to see results on a per-browser level: <a href="http://jsperf.com/getelementbyid-vs-jquery-id" target="_blank">http://jsperf.com/getelementbyid-vs-jquery-id</a>. Now of course, we have to keep in mind that jQuery (and Sizzle - its selector engine) are doing a lot more behind the scenes to optimize our query (and that a jQuery object, not just a DOM node is returned). </p>

<p>The challenge with this particular Facade is that in order to provide an elegant selector function capable of accepting and parsing multiple types of queries, there is an implicit cost of abstraction. The user isn't required to access <code>jQuery.getById("identifier")</code> or <code>jQuery.getByClass("identifier")</code> and so on. That said, the trade-off in performance has been tested in practice over the years and given the success of jQuery, a simple Facade actually worked out very well for the team.</p>

<p>When using the pattern, try to be aware of any performance costs involved and make a call on whether they are worth the level of abstraction offered.</p>



<h2 id="factorypatternjavascript">
  <a href="#factorypatternjavascript" target="_blank">#</a>
  The Factory Pattern
</h2>


<p>The Factory pattern is another creational pattern concerned with the notion of creating objects. Where it differs from the other patterns in its category is that it doesn't explicitly require us to use a constructor. Instead, a Factory can provide a generic interface for creating objects, where we can specify the type of factory object we wish to be created.</p>

<p>Imagine that we have a UI factory where we are asked to create a type of UI component. Rather than creating this component directly using the <code>new</code> operator or via another creational constructor, we ask a Factory object for a new component instead. We inform the Factory what type of object is required (e.g "Button", "Panel") and it instantiates this, returning it to us for use.</p>

<p>This is particularly useful if the object creation process is relatively complex, e.g. if it strongly depends on dynamic factors or application configuration.</p>

<p>Examples of this pattern can be found in UI libraries such as ExtJS where the methods for creating objects or components may be further subclassed.</p>

<p>The following is an example that builds upon our previous snippets using the Constructor pattern logic to define cars. It demonstrates how a Vehicle <em>Factory</em> may be implemented using the Factory pattern:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_713655"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Types.js - Constructors used behind the scenes</code></div><div><code>// A constructor for defining new cars</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>Car( options ) {</code></div><div><code>// some defaults</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.doors = options.doors || 4;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.state = options.state || </code><code>"brand new"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.color = options.color || </code><code>"silver"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// A constructor for defining new trucks</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>Truck( options){</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.state = options.state || </code><code>"used"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.wheelSize = options.wheelSize || </code><code>"large"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.color = options.color || </code><code>"blue"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// FactoryExample.js</code></div><div><code>// Define a skeleton vehicle factory</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>VehicleFactory() {}</code></div><div><code>// Define the prototypes and utilities for this factory</code></div><div><code>// Our default vehicleClass is Car</code></div><div><code>VehicleFactory.prototype.vehicleClass = Car;</code></div><div><code>// Our Factory method for creating new Vehicle instances</code></div><div><code>VehicleFactory.prototype.createVehicle = </code><code>function</code> <code>( options ) {</code></div><div><code>switch</code><code>(options.vehicleType){</code></div><div><code>case</code> <code>"car"</code><code>:</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.vehicleClass = Car;</code></div><div><code>break</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>case</code> <code>"truck"</code><code>:</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.vehicleClass = Truck;</code></div><div><code>break</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>//defaults to VehicleFactory.prototype.vehicleClass (Car)</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>new</code> <code>this</code><code>.vehicleClass( options );</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// Create an instance of our factory that makes cars</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>carFactory = </code><code>new</code> <code>VehicleFactory();</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>car = carFactory.createVehicle( {</code></div><div><code>vehicleType: </code><code>"car"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>color: </code><code>"yellow"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>doors: 6 } );</code></div><div><code>// Test to confirm our car was created using the vehicleClass/prototype Car</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: true</code></div><div><code>console.log( car </code><code>instanceof</code> <code>Car );</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: Car object of color "yellow", doors: 6 in a "brand new" state</code></div><div><code>console.log( car );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p><strong>Approach #1: Modify a VehicleFactory instance to use the Truck class</strong></p>

<div><div id="highlighter_48157"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>movingTruck = carFactory.createVehicle( {</code></div><div><code>vehicleType: </code><code>"truck"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>state: </code><code>"like new"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>color: </code><code>"red"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>wheelSize: </code><code>"small"</code> <code>} );</code></div><div><code>// Test to confirm our truck was created with the vehicleClass/prototype Truck</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: true</code></div><div><code>console.log( movingTruck </code><code>instanceof</code> <code>Truck );</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: Truck object of color "red", a "like new" state</code></div><div><code>// and a "small" wheelSize</code></div><div><code>console.log( movingTruck );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p><strong>Approach #2: Subclass VehicleFactory to create a factory class that builds Trucks</strong></p>

<div><div id="highlighter_439497"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>function</code> <code>TruckFactory () {}</code></div><div><code>TruckFactory.prototype = </code><code>new</code> <code>VehicleFactory();</code></div><div><code>TruckFactory.prototype.vehicleClass = Truck;</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>truckFactory = </code><code>new</code> <code>TruckFactory();</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>myBigTruck = truckFactory.createVehicle( {</code></div><div><code>state: </code><code>"omg..so bad."</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>color: </code><code>"pink"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>wheelSize: </code><code>"so big"</code> <code>} );</code></div><div><code>// Confirms that myBigTruck was created with the prototype Truck</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: true</code></div><div><code>console.log( myBigTruck </code><code>instanceof</code> <code>Truck );</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: Truck object with the color "pink", wheelSize "so big"</code></div><div><code>// and state "omg. so bad"</code></div><div><code>console.log( myBigTruck );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<h3>When To Use The Factory Pattern</h3>

<p>The Factory pattern can be especially useful when applied to the following situations:
</p><ul>
  <li>When our object or component setup involves a high level of complexity</li>
  <li>When we need to easily generate different instances of objects depending on the environment we are in</li>
  <li>When we're working with many small objects or components that share the same properties</li>
  <li>When composing objects with instances of other objects that need only satisfy an API contract (aka, duck typing) to work. This is useful for decoupling.</li>
</ul>
<h3>When Not To Use The Factory Pattern</h3>

<p>
When applied to the wrong type of problem, this pattern can introduce an unnecessarily great deal of complexity to an application. Unless providing an interface for object creation is a design goal for the library or framework we are writing, I would suggest sticking to explicit constructors to avoid the unnecessary overhead.</p>

<p>Due to the fact that the process of object creation is effectively abstracted behind an interface, this can also introduce problems with unit testing depending on just how complex this process might be.</p>

<h3>Abstract Factories</h3>

<p>It is also useful to be aware of the <strong>Abstract Factory</strong> pattern, which aims to encapsulate a group of individual factories with a common goal. It separates the details of implementation of a set of objects from their general usage.</p>

<p>An Abstract Factory should be used where a system must be independent from the way the objects it creates are generated or it needs to work with multiple types of objects.</p>

<p>An example which is both simple and easier to understand is a vehicle factory, which defines ways to get or register vehicles types. The abstract factory can be named abstractVehicleFactory. The Abstract factory will allow the definition of types of vehicle like "car" or "truck" and concrete factories will implement only classes that fulfill the vehicle contract (e.g <code>Vehicle.prototype.drive</code> and <code>Vehicle.prototype.breakDown</code>).</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_818457"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>abstractVehicleFactory = (</code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>// Storage for our vehicle types</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>types = {};</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>getVehicle: </code><code>function</code> <code>( type, customizations ) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>Vehicle = types[type];</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>(Vehicle ? </code><code>new</code> <code>Vehicle(customizations) : </code><code>null</code><code>);</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>registerVehicle: </code><code>function</code> <code>( type, Vehicle ) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>proto = Vehicle.prototype;</code></div><div><code>// only register classes that fulfill the vehicle contract</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( proto.drive && proto.breakDown ) {</code></div><div><code>types[type] = Vehicle;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>abstractVehicleFactory;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>})();</code></div><div><code>// Usage:</code></div><div><code>abstractVehicleFactory.registerVehicle( </code><code>"car"</code><code>, Car );</code></div><div><code>abstractVehicleFactory.registerVehicle( </code><code>"truck"</code><code>, Truck );</code></div><div><code>// Instantiate a new car based on the abstract vehicle type</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>car = abstractVehicleFactory.getVehicle( </code><code>"car"</code><code>, {</code></div><div><code>color: </code><code>"lime green"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>state: </code><code>"like new"</code> <code>} );</code></div><div><code>// Instantiate a new truck in a similar manner</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>truck = abstractVehicleFactory.getVehicle( </code><code>"truck"</code><code>, {</code></div><div><code>wheelSize: </code><code>"medium"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>color: </code><code>"neon yellow"</code> <code>} );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>


<h2 id="mixinpatternjavascript">
  <a href="#mixinpatternjavascript" target="_blank">#</a>
  The Mixin Pattern
</h2>

<p>In traditional programming languages such as C++ and Lisp, Mixins are classes which offer functionality that can be easily inherited by a sub-class or group of sub-classes for the purpose of function re-use.</p>


<h2>Sub-classing</h2>

<p>For developers unfamiliar with sub-classing, we will go through a brief beginners primer on them before diving into Mixins and Decorators further.</p>

<p><em>Sub-classing</em> is a term that refers to inheriting properties for a new object from a base or <em>superclass</em> object. In traditional object-oriented programming, a class <code>B</code> is able to extend another class <code>A</code>. Here we consider <code>A</code> a superclass and <code>B</code> a subclass of <code>A</code>. As such, all instances of <code>B</code> inherit the methods from <code>A</code>. <code>B</code> is however still able to define its own methods, including those that override methods originally defined by <code>A</code>.</p>

<p>Should <code>B</code> need to invoke a method in <code>A</code> that has been overridden, we refer to this as method chaining. Should <code>B</code> need to invoke the constructor <code>A</code> (the superclass), we call this constructor chaining.</p>

<p>In order to demonstrate sub-classing, we first need a base object that can have new instances of itself created. let's model this around the concept of a person.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_845441"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>Person = </code><code>function</code><code>( firstName, lastName ){</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.firstName = firstName;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.lastName = lastName;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.gender = </code><code>"male"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Next, we'll want to specify a new class (object) that's a subclass of the existing <code>Person</code> object. Let us imagine we want to add distinct properties to distinguish a <code>Person</code> from a <code>Superhero</code> whilst inheriting the properties of the <code>Person</code> "superclass". As superheroes share many common traits with normal people (e.g. name, gender), this should hopefully illustrate how sub-classing works adequately.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_679430"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// a new instance of Person can then easily be created as follows:</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>clark = </code><code>new</code> <code>Person( </code><code>"Clark"</code><code>, </code><code>"Kent"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// Define a subclass constructor for for "Superhero":</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>Superhero = </code><code>function</code><code>( firstName, lastName, powers ){</code></div><div><code>// Invoke the superclass constructor on the new object</code></div><div><code>// then use .call() to invoke the constructor as a method of</code></div><div><code>// the object to be initialized.</code></div><div><code>Person.call( </code><code>this</code><code>, firstName, lastName );</code></div><div><code>// Finally, store their powers, a new array of traits not found in a normal "Person"</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.powers = powers;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>Superhero.prototype = Object.create( Person.prototype );</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>superman = </code><code>new</code> <code>Superhero( </code><code>"Clark"</code><code>, </code><code>"Kent"</code><code>, [</code><code>"flight"</code><code>,</code><code>"heat-vision"</code><code>] );</code></div><div><code>console.log( superman );</code></div><div><code>// Outputs Person attributes as well as powers</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>The <code>Superhero</code> constructor creates an object which descends from <code>Person</code>. Objects of this type have attributes of the objects that are above it in the chain and if we had set default values in the <code>Person</code> object, <code>Superhero</code> is capable of overriding any inherited values with values specific to it's object.</p>

<h2>Mixins</h2>

<p>In JavaScript, we can look at inheriting from Mixins as a means of collecting functionality through extension. Each new object we define has a prototype from which it can inherit further properties. Prototypes can inherit from other object prototypes but, even more importantly, can define properties for any number of object instances. We can leverage this fact to promote function re-use.</p>

<p>Mixins allow objects to borrow (or inherit) functionality from them with a minimal amount of complexity. As the pattern works well with JavaScripts object prototypes, it gives us a fairly flexible way to share functionality from not just one Mixin, but effectively many through multiple inheritance.</p>

<p>They can be viewed as objects with attributes and methods that can be easily shared across a number of other object prototypes. Imagine that we define a Mixin containing utility functions in a standard object literal as follows:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_939055"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>myMixins = {</code></div><div><code>moveUp: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"move up"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>moveDown: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"move down"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>stop: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"stop! in the name of love!"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>We can then easily extend the prototype of existing constructor functions to include this behavior using a helper such as the Underscore.js <code>_.extend()</code> method:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_248539"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// A skeleton carAnimator constructor</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>CarAnimator(){</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.moveLeft = </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"move left"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// A skeleton personAnimator constructor</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>PersonAnimator(){</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.moveRandomly = </code><code>function</code><code>(){ </code><code>/*..*/</code> <code>};</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Extend both constructors with our Mixin</code></div><div><code>_.extend( CarAnimator.prototype, myMixins );</code></div><div><code>_.extend( PersonAnimator.prototype, myMixins );</code></div><div><code>// Create a new instance of carAnimator</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>myAnimator = </code><code>new</code> <code>CarAnimator();</code></div><div><code>myAnimator.moveLeft();</code></div><div><code>myAnimator.moveDown();</code></div><div><code>myAnimator.stop();</code></div><div><code>// Outputs:</code></div><div><code>// move left</code></div><div><code>// move down</code></div><div><code>// stop! in the name of love!</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>As we can see, this allows us to easily "mix" in common behaviour into object constructors fairly trivially. </p>

<p>In the next example, we have two constructors: a Car and a Mixin. What we're going to do is augment (another way of saying extend) the Car so that it can inherit specific methods defined in the Mixin, namely <code>driveForward()</code> and <code>driveBackward()</code>. This time we won't be using Underscore.js.</p>

<p>Instead, this example will demonstrate how to augment a constructor to include functionality without the need to duplicate this process for every constructor function we may have.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_388267"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Define a simple Car constructor</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>Car = </code><code>function</code> <code>( settings ) {</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.model = settings.model || </code><code>"no model provided"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.color = settings.color || </code><code>"no colour provided"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// Mixin</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>Mixin = </code><code>function</code> <code>() {};</code></div><div><code>Mixin.prototype = {</code></div><div><code>driveForward: </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"drive forward"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>driveBackward: </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"drive backward"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>driveSideways: </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"drive sideways"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// Extend an existing object with a method from another</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>augment( receivingClass, givingClass ) {</code></div><div><code>// only provide certain methods</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( arguments[2] ) {</code></div><div><code>for</code> <code>( </code><code>var</code> <code>i = 2, len = arguments.length; i &lt; len; i++ ) {</code></div><div><code>receivingClass.prototype[arguments[i]] = givingClass.prototype[arguments[i]];</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// provide all methods</code></div><div><code>else</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>for</code> <code>( </code><code>var</code> <code>methodName </code><code>in</code> <code>givingClass.prototype ) {</code></div><div><code>// check to make sure the receiving class doesn't</code></div><div><code>// have a method of the same name as the one currently</code></div><div><code>// being processed</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( !Object.hasOwnProperty.call(receivingClass.prototype, methodName) ) {</code></div><div><code>receivingClass.prototype[methodName] = givingClass.prototype[methodName];</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Alternatively (check prototype chain as well):</code></div><div><code>// if ( !receivingClass.prototype[methodName] ) {</code></div><div><code>// receivingClass.prototype[methodName] = givingClass.prototype[methodName];</code></div><div><code>// }</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Augment the Car constructor to include "driveForward" and "driveBackward"</code></div><div><code>augment( Car, Mixin, </code><code>"driveForward"</code><code>, </code><code>"driveBackward"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// Create a new Car</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>myCar = </code><code>new</code> <code>Car({</code></div><div><code>model: </code><code>"Ford Escort"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>color: </code><code>"blue"</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>// Test to make sure we now have access to the methods</code></div><div><code>myCar.driveForward();</code></div><div><code>myCar.driveBackward();</code></div><div><code>// Outputs:</code></div><div><code>// drive forward</code></div><div><code>// drive backward</code></div><div><code>// We can also augment Car to include all functions from our mixin</code></div><div><code>// by not explicitly listing a selection of them</code></div><div><code>augment( Car, Mixin );</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>mySportsCar = </code><code>new</code> <code>Car({</code></div><div><code>model: </code><code>"Porsche"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>color: </code><code>"red"</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>mySportsCar.driveSideways();</code></div><div><code>// Outputs:</code></div><div><code>// drive sideways</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<h3>Advantages & Disadvantages</h3>

<p>Mixins assist in decreasing functional repetition and increasing function re-use in a system. Where an application is likely to require shared behaviour across object instances, we can easily avoid any duplication by maintaining this shared functionality in a Mixin and thus focusing on implementing only the functionality in our system which is truly distinct.</p>

<p>That said, the downsides to Mixins are a little more debatable. Some developers feel that injecting functionality into an object prototype is a bad idea as it leads to both prototype pollution and a level of uncertainty regarding the origin of our functions. In large systems this may well be the case.</p>

<p>I would argue that strong documentation can assist in minimizing the amount of confusion regarding the source of mixed in functions, but as with every pattern, if care is taken during implementation we should be okay.</p>

<h2 id="decoratorpatternjavascript">The Decorator Pattern</h2>


<p>Decorators are a structural design pattern that aim to promote code re-use. Similar to Mixins, they can be considered another viable alternative to object sub-classing.</p>

<p>Classically, Decorators offered the ability to add behaviour to existing classes in a system dynamically. The idea was that the <em>decoration</em> itself wasn't essential to the base functionality of the class, otherwise it would be baked into the <em>superclass</em> itself.</p>

<p>They can be used to modify existing systems where we wish to add additional features to objects without the need to heavily modify the underlying code using them. A common reason why developers use them is their applications may contain features requiring a large quantity of distinct types of object. Imagine having to define hundreds of different object constructors for say, a JavaScript game.</p>

<p>The object constructors could represent distinct player types, each with differing capabilities. A <em>Lord of the Rings</em> game could require constructors for <code>Hobbit</code>, <code>Elf</code>, <code>Orc</code>, <code>Wizard</code>, <code>Mountain Giant</code>, <code>Stone Giant</code> and so on, but there could easily be hundreds of these. If we then factored in capabilities, imagine having to create sub-classes for each combination of capability type e.g <code>HobbitWithRing</code>,<code>HobbitWithSword</code>, <code>HobbitWithRingAndSword</code> and so on.This isn't very practical and certainly isn't manageable when we factor in a growing number of different abilities.</p>

<p>The Decorator pattern isn't heavily tied to how objects are created but instead focuses on the problem of extending their functionality. Rather than just relying on prototypal inheritance, we work with a single base object and progressively add decorator objects which provide the additional capabilities. The idea is that rather than sub-classing, we add (decorate) properties or methods to a base object so it's a little more streamlined.</p>

<p>Adding new attributes to objects in JavaScript is a very straight-forward process so with this in mind, a very simplistic decorator may be implemented as follows:</p>

<h3>Example 1: Decorating Constructors With New Functionality</h3>

<div><div id="highlighter_139502"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// A vehicle constructor</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>Vehicle( vehicleType ){</code></div><div><code>// some sane defaults</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.vehicleType = vehicleType || </code><code>"car"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.model = </code><code>"default"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.license = </code><code>"00000-000"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Test instance for a basic vehicle</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>testInstance = </code><code>new</code> <code>Vehicle( </code><code>"car"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>console.log( testInstance );</code></div><div><code>// Outputs:</code></div><div><code>// vehicle: car, model:default, license: 00000-000</code></div><div><code>// Lets create a new instance of vehicle, to be decorated</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>truck = </code><code>new</code> <code>Vehicle( </code><code>"truck"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// New functionality we're decorating vehicle with</code></div><div><code>truck.setModel = </code><code>function</code><code>( modelName ){</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.model = modelName;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>truck.setColor = </code><code>function</code><code>( color ){</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.color = color;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// Test the value setters and value assignment works correctly</code></div><div><code>truck.setModel( </code><code>"CAT"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>truck.setColor( </code><code>"blue"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>console.log( truck );</code></div><div><code>// Outputs:</code></div><div><code>// vehicle:truck, model:CAT, color: blue</code></div><div><code>// Demonstrate "vehicle" is still unaltered</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>secondInstance = </code><code>new</code> <code>Vehicle( </code><code>"car"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>console.log( secondInstance );</code></div><div><code>// Outputs:</code></div><div><code>// vehicle: car, model:default, license: 00000-000</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>This type of simplistic implementation is functional, but it doesn't really demonstrate all of the strengths Decorators have to offer. For this, we're first going to go through my variation of the Coffee example from an excellent book called <em>Head First Design Patterns</em> by Freeman, Sierra and Bates, which is modeled around a Macbook purchase.</p>

<h3>Example 2: Decorating Objects With Multiple Decorators</h3>
<div><div id="highlighter_633748"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// The constructor to decorate</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>MacBook() {</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.cost = </code><code>function</code> <code>() { </code><code>return</code> <code>997; };</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.screenSize = </code><code>function</code> <code>() { </code><code>return</code> <code>11.6; };</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Decorator 1</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>memory( macbook ) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>v = macbook.cost();</code></div><div><code>macbook.cost = </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>v + 75;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Decorator 2</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>engraving( macbook ){</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>v = macbook.cost();</code></div><div><code>macbook.cost = </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>v + 200;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Decorator 3</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>insurance( macbook ){</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>v = macbook.cost();</code></div><div><code>macbook.cost = </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>v + 250;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>mb = </code><code>new</code> <code>MacBook();</code></div><div><code>memory( mb );</code></div><div><code>engraving( mb );</code></div><div><code>insurance( mb );</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: 1522</code></div><div><code>console.log( mb.cost() );</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: 11.6</code></div><div><code>console.log( mb.screenSize() );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>In the above example, our Decorators are overriding the <code>MacBook()</code> super-class objects <code>.cost()</code> function to return the current price of the <code>Macbook</code> plus the cost of the upgrade being specified.</p>

<p>It's considered a decoration as the original <code>Macbook</code> objects constructor methods which are not overridden (e.g. <code>screenSize()</code>) as well as any other properties which we may define as a part of the <code>Macbook</code> remain unchanged and intact.</p>

<p>There isn't really a defined <em>interface</em> in the above example and we're shifting away the responsibility of ensuring an object meets an interface when moving from the creator to the receiver.</p>

<h2>Pseudo-classical Decorators</h2>

<p>We're now going to examine a variation of the Decorator first presented in a JavaScript form in <em>Pro JavaScript Design Patterns</em> (PJDP) by Dustin Diaz and Ross Harmes.</p>

<p>Unlike some of the examples from earlier, Diaz and Harmes stick more closely to how decorators are implemented in other programming languages (such as Java or C++) using the concept of an "interface", which we will define in more detail shortly.</p>

<p><strong>Note:</strong> This particular variation of the Decorator pattern is provided for reference purposes. If finding it overly complex, I recommend opting for one of the simpler implementations covered earlier.</p>

<h3>Interfaces</h3>

<p>PJDP describes the Decorator as a pattern that is used to transparently wrap objects inside other objects of the same interface. An interface is a way of defining the methods an object <strong>should</strong> have, however, it doesn't actually directly specify how those methods should be implemented.</p>

<p>They can also indicate what parameters the methods take, but this is considered optional.</p>

<p>So, why would we use an interface in JavaScript? The idea is that they're self-documenting and promote reusability. In theory, interfaces also make code more stable by ensuring changes to them must also be made to the objects implementing them.</p>

<p>Below is an example of an implementation of interfaces in JavaScript using duck-typing - an approach that helps determine whether an object is an instance of constructor/object based on the methods it implements.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_171019"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Create interfaces using a pre-defined Interface</code></div><div><code>// constructor that accepts an interface name and</code></div><div><code>// skeleton methods to expose.</code></div><div><code>// In our reminder example summary() and placeOrder()</code></div><div><code>// represent functionality the interface should</code></div><div><code>// support</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>reminder = </code><code>new</code> <code>Interface( </code><code>"List"</code><code>, [</code><code>"summary"</code><code>, </code><code>"placeOrder"</code><code>] );</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>properties = {</code></div><div><code>name: </code><code>"Remember to buy the milk"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>date: </code><code>"05/06/2016"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>actions:{</code></div><div><code>summary: </code><code>function</code> <code>(){</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>"Remember to buy the milk, we are almost out!"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>placeOrder: </code><code>function</code> <code>(){</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>"Ordering milk from your local grocery store"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// Now create a constructor implementing the above properties</code></div><div><code>// and methods</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>Todo( config ){</code></div><div><code>// State the methods we expect to be supported</code></div><div><code>// as well as the Interface instance being checked</code></div><div><code>// against</code></div><div><code>Interface.ensureImplements( config.actions, reminder );</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.name = config.name;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.methods = config.actions;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Create a new instance of our Todo constructor</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>todoItem = </code><code>new</code> <code>Todo( properties );</code></div><div><code>// Finally test to make sure these function correctly</code></div><div><code>console.log( todoItem.methods.summary() );</code></div><div><code>console.log( todoItem.methods.placeOrder() );</code></div><div><code>// Outputs:</code></div><div><code>// Remember to buy the milk, we are almost out!</code></div><div><code>// Ordering milk from your local grocery store</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>In the above, <code>Interface.ensureImplements</code> provides strict functionality checking and code for both this and the <code>Interface</code> constructor can be found <a href="https://gist.github.com/1057989" target="_blank">here</a>.</p>

<p>The biggest problem with interfaces is that, as there isn't built-in support for them in JavaScript, there is a danger of us attempting to emulate a feature of another language that may not be an ideal fit. Lightweight interfaces can be used without a great performance cost however and we will next look at <em>Abstract</em> Decorators using this same concept.</p>

<h3>Abstract Decorators</h3>

<p>To demonstrate the structure of this version of the Decorator pattern, we're going to imagine we have a superclass that models a <code>Macbook</code> once again and a store that allows us to "decorate" our Macbook with a number of enhancements for an additional fee.</p>

<p>Enhancements can include upgrades to 4GB or 8GB Ram, engraving, Parallels or a case. Now if we were to model this using an individual sub-class for each combination of enhancement options, it might look something like this:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_543466"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>Macbook = </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>//...</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>var</code>  <code>MacbookWith4GBRam = </code><code>function</code><code>(){},</code></div><div><code>MacbookWith8GBRam = </code><code>function</code><code>(){},</code></div><div><code>MacbookWith4GBRamAndEngraving = </code><code>function</code><code>(){},</code></div><div><code>MacbookWith8GBRamAndEngraving = </code><code>function</code><code>(){},</code></div><div><code>MacbookWith8GBRamAndParallels = </code><code>function</code><code>(){},</code></div><div><code>MacbookWith4GBRamAndParallels = </code><code>function</code><code>(){},</code></div><div><code>MacbookWith8GBRamAndParallelsAndCase = </code><code>function</code><code>(){},</code></div><div><code>MacbookWith4GBRamAndParallelsAndCase = </code><code>function</code><code>(){},</code></div><div><code>MacbookWith8GBRamAndParallelsAndCaseAndInsurance = </code><code>function</code><code>(){},</code></div><div><code>MacbookWith4GBRamAndParallelsAndCaseAndInsurance = </code><code>function</code><code>(){};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>and so on.</p>
<p>This would be an impractical solution as a new subclass would be required for every possible combination of enhancements that are available. As we would prefer to keep things simple without maintaining a large set of subclasses, let's look at how decorators may be used to solve this problem better.</p>

<p>Rather than requiring all of the combinations we saw earlier, we should simply have to create five new decorator classes. Methods that are called on these enhancement classes would be passed on to our <code>Macbook</code> class.</p>

<p>In our next example, decorators transparently wrap around their components and can interestingly be interchanged as they use the same interface.</p>

<p>Here's the interface we're going to define for the Macbook:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_347119"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>Macbook = </code><code>new</code> <code>Interface( </code><code>"Macbook"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>[</code><code>"addEngraving"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>"addParallels"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>"add4GBRam"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>"add8GBRam"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>"addCase"</code><code>]);</code></div><div><code>// A Macbook Pro might thus be represented as follows:</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>MacbookPro = </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>// implements Macbook</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>MacbookPro.prototype = {</code></div><div><code>addEngraving: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>addParallels: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>add4GBRam: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>add8GBRam:</code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>addCase: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>getPrice: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>// Base price</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>900.00;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>To make it easier for us to add as many more options as needed later on, an Abstract Decorator class is defined with default methods required to implement the <code>Macbook</code> interface, which the rest of the options will sub-class. Abstract Decorators ensure that we can decorate a base class independently with as many decorators as needed in different combinations (remember the example earlier?) without needing to derive a class for every possible combination.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_543618"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Macbook decorator abstract decorator class</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>MacbookDecorator = </code><code>function</code><code>( macbook ){</code></div><div><code>Interface.ensureImplements( macbook, Macbook );</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.macbook = macbook;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>MacbookDecorator.prototype = {</code></div><div><code>addEngraving: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.macbook.addEngraving();</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>addParallels: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.macbook.addParallels();</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>add4GBRam: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.macbook.add4GBRam();</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>add8GBRam:</code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.macbook.add8GBRam();</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>addCase: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.macbook.addCase();</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>getPrice: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.macbook.getPrice();</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>What's happening in the above sample is that the <code>Macbook</code> Decorator accepts an object (a Macbook) to use as our base component. It's using the <code>Macbook</code> interface we defined earlier and for each method is just calling the same method on the component. We can now create our option classes for what can be added, just by using the <code>Macbook</code> Decorator.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_823383"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// First, define a way to extend an object a</code></div><div><code>// with the properties in object b. We'll use</code></div><div><code>// this shortly!</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>extend( a, b ){</code></div><div><code>for</code><code>( </code><code>var</code> <code>key </code><code>in</code> <code>b )</code></div><div><code>if</code><code>( b.hasOwnProperty(key) )</code></div><div><code>a[key] = b[key];</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>a;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>CaseDecorator = </code><code>function</code><code>( macbook ){</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.macbook = macbook;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// Let's now extend (decorate) the CaseDecorator</code></div><div><code>// with a MacbookDecorator</code></div><div><code>extend( CaseDecorator, MacbookDecorator );</code></div><div><code>CaseDecorator.prototype.addCase = </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.macbook.addCase() + </code><code>"Adding case to macbook"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>CaseDecorator.prototype.getPrice = </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.macbook.getPrice() + 45.00;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>What we're doing here is overriding the <code>addCase()</code> and <code>getPrice()</code> methods that need to be decorated and we're achieving this by first calling these methods on the original <code>macbook</code> and then simply appending a string or numeric value (e.g 45.00) to them accordingly.</p>

<p>As there's been quite a lot of information presented in this section so far, let's try to bring it all together in a single example that will hopefully highlight what we have learned.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_434501"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Instantiation of the macbook</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>myMacbookPro = </code><code>new</code> <code>MacbookPro();</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: 900.00</code></div><div><code>console.log( myMacbookPro.getPrice() );</code></div><div><code>// Decorate the macbook</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>decoratedMacbookPro = </code><code>new</code> <code>CaseDecorator( myMacbookPro );</code></div><div><code>// This will return 945.00</code></div><div><code>console.log( decoratedMacbookPro.getPrice() );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>As decorators are able to modify objects dynamically, they're a perfect pattern for changing existing systems. Occasionally, it's just simpler to create decorators around an object versus the trouble of maintaining individual sub-classes for each object type. This makes maintaining applications that may require a large number of sub-classed objects significantly more straight-forward.</p>

<p>A functional version of this example can be found on <a href="http://jsbin.com/UMEJaXu/1/edit" target="_blank">JSBin</a>.</p>

<h2>Decorators With jQuery</h2>

<p>As with other patterns we've covered, there are also examples of the Decorator pattern that can be implemented with jQuery. <code>jQuery.extend()</code> allows us to extend (or merge) two or more objects (and their properties) together into a single object at run-time.</p>

<p>In this scenario, a target object can be decorated with new functionality without necessarily breaking or overriding existing methods in the source/superclass object (although this can be done).</p>

<p>In the following example, we define three objects: defaults, options and settings. The aim of the task is to decorate the <code>defaults</code> object with additional functionality found in <code>optionssettings</code>. We must:</p>

<p>(a) Leave "defaults" in an untouched state where we don't lose the ability to access the properties or functions found in it a later point (b) Gain the ability to use the decorated properties and functions found in "options"</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_436904"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>decoratorApp = decoratorApp || {};</code></div><div><code>// define the objects we're going to use</code></div><div><code>decoratorApp = {</code></div><div><code>defaults: {</code></div><div><code>validate: </code><code>false</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>limit: 5,</code></div><div><code>name: </code><code>"foo"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>welcome: </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"welcome!"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>options: {</code></div><div><code>validate: </code><code>true</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>name: </code><code>"bar"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>helloWorld: </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"hello world"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>settings: {},</code></div><div><code>printObj: </code><code>function</code> <code>( obj ) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>arr = [],</code></div><div><code>next;</code></div><div><code>$.each( obj, </code><code>function</code> <code>( key, val ) {</code></div><div><code>next = key + </code><code>": "</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>next += $.isPlainObject(val) ? printObj( val ) : val;</code></div><div><code>arr.push( next );</code></div><div><code>} );</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>"{ "</code> <code>+ arr.join(</code><code>", "</code><code>) + </code><code>" }"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// merge defaults and options, without modifying defaults explicitly</code></div><div><code>decoratorApp.settings = $.extend({}, decoratorApp.defaults, decoratorApp.options);</code></div><div><code>// what we have done here is decorated defaults in a way that provides</code></div><div><code>// access to the properties and functionality it has to offer (as well as</code></div><div><code>// that of the decorator "options"). defaults itself is left unchanged</code></div><div><code>$(</code><code>"#log"</code><code>)</code></div><div><code>.append( decoratorApp.printObj(decoratorApp.settings) +</code></div><div><code>+ decoratorApp.printObj(decoratorApp.options) +</code></div><div><code>+ decoratorApp.printObj(decoratorApp.defaults));</code></div><div><code>// settings -- { validate: true, limit: 5, name: bar, welcome: function (){ console.log( "welcome!" ); },</code></div><div><code>// helloWorld: function (){ console.log( "hello world" ); } }</code></div><div><code>// options -- { validate: true, name: bar, helloWorld: function (){ console.log( "hello world" ); } }</code></div><div><code>// defaults -- { validate: false, limit: 5, name: foo, welcome: function (){ console.log("welcome!"); } }</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<h2>Advantages & Disadvantages</h2>

<p>Developers enjoy using this pattern as it can be used transparently and is also fairly flexible - as we've seen, objects can be wrapped or "decorated" with new behavior and then continue to be used without needing to worry about the base object being modified. In a broader context, this pattern also avoids us needing to rely on large numbers of subclasses to get the same benefits.</p>

<p>There are however drawbacks that we should be aware of when implementing the pattern. If poorly managed, it can significantly complicate our application architecture as it introduces many small, but similar objects into our namespace. The concern here is that in addition to becoming hard to manage, other developers unfamiliar with the pattern may have a hard time grasping why it's being used.</p>

<p>Sufficient commenting or pattern research should assist with the latter, however as long as we keep a handle on how widespread we use the decorator in our applications we should be fine on both counts.</p>

<h1 id="detailflyweight">
  <a href="#detailflyweight" target="_blank">#</a>
  Flyweight
</h1>
<p>The Flyweight pattern is a classical structural solution for optimizing code that is repetitive, slow and inefficiently shares data. It aims to minimize the use of memory in an application by sharing as much data as possible with related objects (e.g application configuration, state and so on).</p>

<p>The pattern was first conceived by Paul Calder and Mark Linton in 1990 and was named after the boxing weight class that includes fighters weighing less than 112lb. The name Flyweight itself is derived from this weight classification as it refers to the small weight (memory footprint) the pattern aims to help us achieve.</p>

<p>In practice, Flyweight data sharing can involve taking several similar objects or data constructs used by a number of objects and placing this data into a single external object. We can pass through this object to those depending on this data, rather than storing identical data across each one.</p>

<h2>Using Flyweights</h2>

<p>There are two ways in which the Flyweight pattern can be applied. The first is at the data-layer, where we deal with the concept of sharing data between large quantities of similar objects stored in memory.</p>

<p>The second is at the DOM-layer where the Flyweight can be used as a central event-manager to avoid attaching event handlers to every child element in a parent container we wish to have some similar behavior.</p>

<p>As the data-layer is where the flyweight pattern is most used traditionally, we'll take a look at this first.</p>

<h2>Flyweights and sharing data</h2>

<p>For this application, there are a few more concepts around the classical Flyweight pattern that we need to be aware of. In the Flyweight pattern there's a concept of two states - intrinsic and extrinsic. Intrinsic information may be required by internal methods in our objects which they absolutely cannot function without. Extrinsic information can however be removed and stored externally.</p>

<p>Objects with the same intrinsic data can be replaced with a single shared object, created by a factory method. This allows us to reduce the overall quantity of implicit data being stored quite significantly.</p>

<p>The benefit of this is that we're able to keep an eye on objects that have already been instantiated so that new copies are only ever created should the intrinsic state differ from the object we already have.</p>

<p>We use a manager to handle the extrinsic states. How this is implemented can vary, but one approach to this to have the manager object contain a central database of the extrinsic states and the flyweight objects which they belong to.</p>


<h2>Implementing Classical Flyweights</h2>

<p>As the Flyweight pattern hasn't been heavily used in JavaScript in recent years, many of the implementations we might use for inspiration come from the Java and C++ worlds.</p>

<p>Our first look at Flyweights in code is my JavaScript implementation of the Java sample of the Flyweight pattern from Wikipedia (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flyweight_pattern" target="_blank">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flyweight_pattern</a>).

</p><p>We will be making use of three types of Flyweight components in this implementation, which are listed below:</p>

<ul>
    <li><strong>Flyweight</strong> corresponds to an interface through which flyweights are able to receive and act on extrinsic states</li>
    <li><strong>Concrete Flyweight</strong> actually implements the Flyweight interface and stores intrinsic state. Concrete Flyweights need to be sharable and capable of manipulating state that is extrinsic</li>
    <li><strong>Flyweight Factory</strong> manages flyweight objects and creates them too. It makes sure that our flyweights are shared and manages them as a group of objects which can be queried if we require individual instances. If an object has been already created in the group it returns it, otherwise it adds a new object to the pool and returns it.</li>
  </ul>
<p>These correspond to the following definitions in our implementation:</p>
<ul>
    <li>CoffeeOrder: Flyweight</li>
    <li>CoffeeFlavor: Concrete Flyweight </li>
    <li>CoffeeOrderContext: Helper</li>
    <li>CoffeeFlavorFactory: Flyweight Factory</li>
    <li>testFlyweight: Utilization of our Flyweights</li>
  </ul>
<h3>Duck punching "implements"</h3>

<p>Duck punching allows us to extend the capabilities of a language or solution without necessarily needing to modify the runtime source. As this next solution requires the use of a Java keyword (<code>implements</code>) for implementing interfaces and isn't found in JavaScript natively, let's first duck punch it.</p>

<p><code>Function.prototype.implementsFor</code> works on an object constructor and will accept a parent class (function) or object and either inherit from this using normal inheritance (for functions) or virtual inheritance (for objects).</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_4127"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Simulate pure virtual inheritance/"implement" keyword for JS</code></div><div><code>Function.prototype.implementsFor = </code><code>function</code><code>( parentClassOrObject ){</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( parentClassOrObject.constructor === Function )</code></div><div><code>{</code></div><div><code>// Normal Inheritance</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.prototype = </code><code>new</code> <code>parentClassOrObject();</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.prototype.constructor = </code><code>this</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.prototype.parent = parentClassOrObject.prototype;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>else</code></div><div><code>{</code></div><div><code>// Pure Virtual Inheritance</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.prototype = parentClassOrObject;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.prototype.constructor = </code><code>this</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.prototype.parent = parentClassOrObject;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>We can use this to patch the lack of an <code>implements</code> keyword by having a function inherit an interface explicitly. Below, <code>CoffeeFlavor</code> implements the <code>CoffeeOrder</code> interface and must contain its interface methods in order for us to assign the functionality powering these implementations to an object.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_410707"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Flyweight object</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>CoffeeOrder = {</code></div><div><code>// Interfaces</code></div><div><code>serveCoffee:</code><code>function</code><code>(context){},</code></div><div><code>getFlavor:</code><code>function</code><code>(){}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// ConcreteFlyweight object that creates ConcreteFlyweight</code></div><div><code>// Implements CoffeeOrder</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>CoffeeFlavor( newFlavor ){</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>flavor = newFlavor;</code></div><div><code>// If an interface has been defined for a feature</code></div><div><code>// implement the feature</code></div><div><code>if</code><code>( </code><code>typeof</code> <code>this</code><code>.getFlavor === </code><code>"function"</code> <code>){</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.getFlavor = </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>flavor;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>if</code><code>( </code><code>typeof</code> <code>this</code><code>.serveCoffee === </code><code>"function"</code> <code>){</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.serveCoffee = </code><code>function</code><code>( context ) {</code></div><div><code>console.log(</code><code>"Serving Coffee flavor "</code></div><div><code>+ flavor</code></div><div><code>+ </code><code>" to table number "</code></div><div><code>+ context.getTable());</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Implement interface for CoffeeOrder</code></div><div><code>CoffeeFlavor.implementsFor( CoffeeOrder );</code></div><div><code>// Handle table numbers for a coffee order</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>CoffeeOrderContext( tableNumber ) {</code></div><div><code>return</code><code>{</code></div><div><code>getTable: </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>tableNumber;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>CoffeeFlavorFactory() {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>flavors = {},</code></div><div><code>length = 0;</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>getCoffeeFlavor: </code><code>function</code> <code>(flavorName) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>flavor = flavors[flavorName];</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>(</code><code>typeof</code> <code>flavor === </code><code>"undefined"</code><code>) {</code></div><div><code>flavor = </code><code>new</code> <code>CoffeeFlavor(flavorName);</code></div><div><code>flavors[flavorName] = flavor;</code></div><div><code>length++;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>flavor;</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>getTotalCoffeeFlavorsMade: </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>length;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Sample usage:</code></div><div><code>// testFlyweight()</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>testFlyweight(){</code></div><div><code>// The flavors ordered.</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>flavors = [],</code></div><div><code>// The tables for the orders.</code></div><div><code>tables = [],</code></div><div><code>// Number of orders made</code></div><div><code>ordersMade = 0,</code></div><div><code>// The CoffeeFlavorFactory instance</code></div><div><code>flavorFactory = </code><code>new</code> <code>CoffeeFlavorFactory();</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>takeOrders( flavorIn, table) {</code></div><div><code>flavors.push( flavorFactory.getCoffeeFlavor( flavorIn ) );</code></div><div><code>tables.push( </code><code>new</code> <code>CoffeeOrderContext( table ) );</code></div><div><code>ordersMade++;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>takeOrders(</code><code>"Cappuccino"</code><code>, 2);</code></div><div><code>takeOrders(</code><code>"Cappuccino"</code><code>, 2);</code></div><div><code>takeOrders(</code><code>"Frappe"</code><code>, 1);</code></div><div><code>takeOrders(</code><code>"Frappe"</code><code>, 1);</code></div><div><code>takeOrders(</code><code>"Xpresso"</code><code>, 1);</code></div><div><code>takeOrders(</code><code>"Frappe"</code><code>, 897);</code></div><div><code>takeOrders(</code><code>"Cappuccino"</code><code>, 97);</code></div><div><code>takeOrders(</code><code>"Cappuccino"</code><code>, 97);</code></div><div><code>takeOrders(</code><code>"Frappe"</code><code>, 3);</code></div><div><code>takeOrders(</code><code>"Xpresso"</code><code>, 3);</code></div><div><code>takeOrders(</code><code>"Cappuccino"</code><code>, 3);</code></div><div><code>takeOrders(</code><code>"Xpresso"</code><code>, 96);</code></div><div><code>takeOrders(</code><code>"Frappe"</code><code>, 552);</code></div><div><code>takeOrders(</code><code>"Cappuccino"</code><code>, 121);</code></div><div><code>takeOrders(</code><code>"Xpresso"</code><code>, 121);</code></div><div><code>for</code> <code>(</code><code>var</code> <code>i = 0; i &lt; ordersMade; ++i) {</code></div><div><code>flavors[i].serveCoffee(tables[i]);</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>console.log(</code><code>" "</code><code>);</code></div><div><code>console.log(</code><code>"total CoffeeFlavor objects made: "</code> <code>+ flavorFactory.getTotalCoffeeFlavorsMade());</code></div><div><code>}</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<h2>Converting code to use the Flyweight pattern</h2>

<p>
Next, let's continue our look at Flyweights by implementing a system to manage all of the books in a library. The important meta-data for each book could probably be broken down as follows:</p>
<ul>

<li>Title</li>
<li>Author</li>
<li>Genre</li>
<li>Page count</li>
<li>Publisher ID</li>

</ul>
<p>
We'll also require the following properties to keep track of which member has checked out a particular book, the date they've checked it out on as well as the expected date of return.
</p>
<ul>
<li>checkoutDate</li>
<li>checkoutMember</li>
<li>dueReturnDate</li>
<li>availability</li>
</ul>
<p>
Each book would thus be represented as follows, prior to any optimization using the Flyweight pattern:
</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_366918"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>Book = </code><code>function</code><code>( id, title, author, genre, pageCount,publisherID, ISBN, checkoutDate, checkoutMember, dueReturnDate,availability ){</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.id = id;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.title = title;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.author = author;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.genre = genre;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.pageCount = pageCount;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.publisherID = publisherID;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.ISBN = ISBN;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.checkoutDate = checkoutDate;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.checkoutMember = checkoutMember;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.dueReturnDate = dueReturnDate;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.availability = availability;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>Book.prototype = {</code></div><div><code>getTitle: </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.title;</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>getAuthor: </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.author;</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>getISBN: </code><code>function</code> <code>(){</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.ISBN;</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>// For brevity, other getters are not shown</code></div><div><code>updateCheckoutStatus: </code><code>function</code><code>( bookID, newStatus, checkoutDate, checkoutMember, newReturnDate ){</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.id = bookID;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.availability = newStatus;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.checkoutDate = checkoutDate;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.checkoutMember = checkoutMember;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.dueReturnDate = newReturnDate;</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>extendCheckoutPeriod: </code><code>function</code><code>( bookID, newReturnDate ){</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.id = bookID;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.dueReturnDate = newReturnDate;</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>isPastDue: </code><code>function</code><code>(bookID){</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>currentDate = </code><code>new</code> <code>Date();</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>currentDate.getTime() &gt; Date.parse( </code><code>this</code><code>.dueReturnDate );</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>
This probably works fine initially for small collections of books, however as the library expands to include a larger inventory with multiple versions and copies of each book available, we may find the management system running slower and slower over time. Using thousands of book objects may overwhelm the available memory, but we can optimize our system using the Flyweight pattern to improve this.</p>
<p>
We can now separate our data into intrinsic and extrinsic states as follows: data relevant to the book object (<code>title</code>, <code>author</code> etc) is intrinsic whilst the checkout data (<code>checkoutMember</code>, <code>dueReturnDate</code> etc) is considered extrinsic. Effectively this means that only one Book object is required for each combination of book properties. it's still a considerable quantity of objects, but significantly fewer than we had previously.
</p>

<p>
The following single instance of our book meta-data combinations will be shared among all of the copies of a book with a particular title.
</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_346433"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Flyweight optimized version</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>Book = </code><code>function</code> <code>( title, author, genre, pageCount, publisherID, ISBN ) {</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.title = title;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.author = author;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.genre = genre;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.pageCount = pageCount;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.publisherID = publisherID;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.ISBN = ISBN;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>
As we can see, the extrinsic states have been removed. Everything to do with library check-outs will be moved to a manager and as the object data is now segmented, a factory can be used for instantiation.
</p>

<h2>A Basic Factory</h2>

<p>
Let's now define a very basic factory. What we're going to have it do is perform a check to see if a book with a particular title has been previously created inside the system; if it has, we'll return it - if not, a new book will be created and stored so that it can be accessed later. This makes sure that we only create a single copy of each unique intrinsic piece of data:
</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_750209"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Book Factory singleton</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>BookFactory = (</code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>existingBooks = {}, existingBook;</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>createBook: </code><code>function</code> <code>( title, author, genre, pageCount, publisherID, ISBN ) {</code></div><div><code>// Find out if a particular book meta-data combination has been created before</code></div><div><code>// !! or (bang bang) forces a boolean to be returned</code></div><div><code>existingBook = existingBooks[ISBN];</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( !!existingBook ) {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>existingBook;</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>else</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>// if not, let's create a new instance of the book and store it</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>book = </code><code>new</code> <code>Book( title, author, genre, pageCount, publisherID, ISBN );</code></div><div><code>existingBooks[ISBN] = book;</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>book;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>})();</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>


<h2>Managing the extrinsic states</h2>

<p>
Next, we need to store the states that were removed from the Book objects somewhere - luckily a manager (which we'll be defining as a Singleton) can be used to encapsulate them. Combinations of a Book object and the library member that's checked them out will be called Book records. Our manager will be storing both and will also include checkout related logic we stripped out during our flyweight optimization of the Book class.
</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_240436"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// BookRecordManager singleton</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>BookRecordManager = (</code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>bookRecordDatabase = {};</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>// add a new book into the library system</code></div><div><code>addBookRecord: </code><code>function</code> <code>( id, title, author, genre, pageCount, publisherID, ISBN, checkoutDate, checkoutMember, dueReturnDate, availability ) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>book = BookFactory.createBook( title, author, genre, pageCount, publisherID, ISBN );</code></div><div><code>bookRecordDatabase[id] =
