Our best bets however until we have specification finalization are AMD (for in-browser modules) and CommonJS (for those on the server).<div>
    <h2>Conclusions</h2>
  </div>
<p>In this section we reviewed several of the options available for writing modular JavaScript using modern module formats.</p>

<p>These formats have a number of advantages over using the module pattern alone including: avoiding the need to manage global variables, better support for static and dynamic dependency management, improved compatibility with script loaders, better compatibility for modules on the server and more.</p>

<p>In short, I recommend trying out what's been suggested in this chapter as these formats offer a great deal of power and flexibility that can significantly assist with better organizing our applications.</p>



<h1 id="designpatternsjquery">
  <a href="#designpatternsjquery" target="_blank">#</a>
  Design Patterns In jQuery
</h1>


<p>jQuery is currently the most popular JavaScript DOM manipulation library and provides an abstracted layer for interacting with the DOM in a safe, cross-browser manner. Interestingly, the library also serves as an example of how design patterns can be effectively used to create an API which is both readable and easy to use.</p>

<p>Whilst in many cases the core contributors that wrote jQuery didn't set out to use specific patterns, they exist there regardless and are useful to learn from. Let's take a look at what some of these patterns are and how they are used in the API.
</p>



<h2 id="compositepatternjquery">
  <a href="#compositepatternjquery" target="_blank">#</a>
  The Composite Pattern
</h2>
<p>
  <strong>The Composite Pattern</strong> describes a group of objects that can be treated in the same way a single instance of an object may be.</p>

  <p>This allows us to treat both individual objects and compositions in a uniform manner, meaning that the same behavior will be applied regardless of whether we're working with one item or a thousand.</p>

  <p>In jQuery, when we're applying methods to an element or collection of elements, we can treat both sets in a uniform manner as both selections return a jQuery object.</p>

  <p>This is demonstrated by the code sample using the jQuery selector below. Here it's possible to add an <code>active</code> class to both selections for a single element (e.g an element with a unique ID) or a group of elements with the same tag name or class, without additional effort:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_922493"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Single elements</code></div><div><code>$( </code><code>"#singleItem"</code> <code>).addClass( </code><code>"active"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>$( </code><code>"#container"</code> <code>).addClass( </code><code>"active"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// Collections of elements</code></div><div><code>$( </code><code>"div"</code> <code>).addClass( </code><code>"active"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>$( </code><code>".item"</code> <code>).addClass( </code><code>"active"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>$( </code><code>"input"</code> <code>).addClass( </code><code>"active"</code> <code>);</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>The jQuery <code>addClass()</code> implementation could either directly use native <em>for</em> loops (or jQuery's <code>jQuery.each()</code>/<code>jQuery.fn.each()</code>) to iterate through a collection in order to apply the method to both single items or groups. Looking through the source we can see this is indeed the case:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_292549"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>addClass: </code><code>function</code><code>( value ) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>classNames, i, l, elem,</code></div><div><code>setClass, c, cl;</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( jQuery.isFunction( value ) ) {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.each(</code><code>function</code><code>( j ) {</code></div><div><code>jQuery( </code><code>this</code> <code>).addClass( value.call(</code><code>this</code><code>, j, </code><code>this</code><code>.className) );</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( value && </code><code>typeof</code> <code>value === </code><code>"string"</code> <code>) {</code></div><div><code>classNames = value.split( rspace );</code></div><div><code>for</code> <code>( i = 0, l = </code><code>this</code><code>.length; i &lt; l; i++ ) {</code></div><div><code>elem = </code><code>this</code><code>[ i ];</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( elem.nodeType === 1 ) {</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( !elem.className && classNames.length === 1 ) {</code></div><div><code>elem.className = value;</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>else</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>setClass = </code><code>" "</code> <code>+ elem.className + </code><code>" "</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>for</code> <code>( c = 0, cl = classNames.length; c &lt; cl; c++ ) {</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( !~setClass.indexOf( </code><code>" "</code> <code>+ classNames[ c ] + </code><code>" "</code> <code>) ) {</code></div><div><code>setClass += classNames[ c ] + </code><code>" "</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>elem.className = jQuery.trim( setClass );</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<h2 id="wrapperpatternjquery"><strong>The Adapter Pattern</strong></h2>
<p>
  <strong>The Adapter Pattern</strong> translates an <em>interface</em> for an object or class into an interface compatible with a specific system.</p>

  <p>Adapters basically allow objects or classes to function together which normally couldn't due to their incompatible interfaces. The adapter translates calls to its interface into calls to the original interface and the code required to achieve this is usually quite minimal.
</p>

<p>One example of an adapter we may have used is the jQuery <code>jQuery.fn.css()</code> method. It helps normalize the interfaces to how styles can be applied across a number of browsers, making it trivial for us to use a simple syntax which is adapted to use what the browser actually supports behind the scenes:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_284640"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Cross browser opacity:</code></div><div><code>// opacity: 0.9; Chrome 4+, FF2+, Saf3.1+, Opera 9+, IE9, iOS 3.2+, Android 2.1+</code></div><div><code>// filter: alpha(opacity=90); IE6-IE8</code></div><div><code>// Setting opacity</code></div><div><code>$( </code><code>".container"</code> <code>).css( { opacity: .5 } );</code></div><div><code>// Getting opacity</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>currentOpacity = $( </code><code>".container"</code> <code>).css(</code><code>'opacity'</code><code>);</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>The corresponding jQuery core cssHook which makes the above possible can be seen below:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_173539"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>get: </code><code>function</code><code>( elem, computed ) {</code></div><div><code>// IE uses filters for opacity</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>ropacity.test( (</code></div><div><code>computed && elem.currentStyle ?</code></div><div><code>elem.currentStyle.filter : elem.style.filter) || </code><code>""</code> <code>) ?</code></div><div><code>( parseFloat( RegExp.$1 ) / 100 ) + </code><code>""</code> <code>:</code></div><div><code>computed ? </code><code>"1"</code> <code>: </code><code>""</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>set: </code><code>function</code><code>( elem, value ) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>style = elem.style,</code></div><div><code>currentStyle = elem.currentStyle,</code></div><div><code>opacity = jQuery.isNumeric( value ) ?</code></div><div><code>"alpha(opacity="</code> <code>+ value * 100 + </code><code>")"</code> <code>: </code><code>""</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>filter = currentStyle && currentStyle.filter || style.filter || </code><code>""</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>// IE has trouble with opacity if it does not have layout</code></div><div><code>// Force it by setting the zoom level</code></div><div><code>style.zoom = 1;</code></div><div><code>// if setting opacity to 1, and no other filters</code></div><div><code>//exist - attempt to remove filter attribute #6652</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( value &gt;= 1 && jQuery.trim( filter.replace( ralpha, </code><code>""</code> <code>) ) === </code><code>""</code> <code>) {</code></div><div><code>// Setting style.filter to null, "" & " " still leave</code></div><div><code>// "filter:" in the cssText if "filter:" is present at all,</code></div><div><code>// clearType is disabled, we want to avoid this style.removeAttribute</code></div><div><code>// is IE Only, but so apparently is this code path...</code></div><div><code>style.removeAttribute( </code><code>"filter"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// if there there is no filter style applied in a css rule, we are done</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( currentStyle && !currentStyle.filter ) {</code></div><div><code>return</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// otherwise, set new filter values</code></div><div><code>style.filter = ralpha.test( filter ) ?</code></div><div><code>filter.replace( ralpha, opacity ) :</code></div><div><code>filter + </code><code>" "</code> <code>+ opacity;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>


<h2 id="facadepatternjquery">
  <a href="#facadepatternjquery" target="_blank">#</a>
  The Facade Pattern
</h2>

<p>As we reviewed earlier in the book, the <strong>Facade Pattern</strong> provides a simpler abstracted interface to a larger (potentially more complex) body of code.</p>

<p>Facades can be frequently found across the jQuery library and provide developers easy access to implementations for handling DOM manipulation, animation and of particular interest, cross-browser Ajax.</p>

<p>The following are facades for jQuery's <code>$.ajax()</code>:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_691735"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$.get( url, data, callback, dataType );</code></div><div><code>$.post( url, data, callback, dataType );</code></div><div><code>$.getJSON( url, data, callback );</code></div><div><code>$.getScript( url, callback );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>These are translated behind the scenes to:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_613561"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// $.get()</code></div><div><code>$.ajax({</code></div><div><code>url: url,</code></div><div><code>data: data,</code></div><div><code>dataType: dataType</code></div><div><code>}).done( callback );</code></div><div><code>// $.post</code></div><div><code>$.ajax({</code></div><div><code>type: </code><code>"POST"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>url: url,</code></div><div><code>data: data,</code></div><div><code>dataType: dataType</code></div><div><code>}).done( callback );</code></div><div><code>// $.getJSON()</code></div><div><code>$.ajax({</code></div><div><code>url: url,</code></div><div><code>dataType: </code><code>"json"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>data: data,</code></div><div><code>}).done( callback );</code></div><div><code>// $.getScript()</code></div><div><code>$.ajax({</code></div><div><code>url: url,</code></div><div><code>dataType: </code><code>"script"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>}).done( callback );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>What's even more interesting is that the above facades are actually facades in their own right, hiding a great deal of complexity behind the scenes.</p>

<p>This is because the <code>jQuery.ajax()</code> implementation in jQuery core is a non-trivial piece of code to say the least. At minimum it normalizes the cross-browser differences between XHR (XMLHttpRequest) and makes it trivial for us to perform common HTTP actions (e.g <code>get</code>, <code>post</code> etc), work with Deferreds and so on.</p>

<p>As it would take an entire chapter to show all of the code related to the above facades, here is instead the code in jQuery core normalizing XHR:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_247741"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Functions to create xhrs</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>createStandardXHR() {</code></div><div><code>try</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>new</code> <code>window.XMLHttpRequest();</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>catch</code><code>( e ) {}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>createActiveXHR() {</code></div><div><code>try</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>new</code> <code>window.ActiveXObject( </code><code>"Microsoft.XMLHTTP"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>catch</code><code>( e ) {}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Create the request object</code></div><div><code>jQuery.ajaxSettings.xhr = window.ActiveXObject ?</code></div><div><code>/* Microsoft failed to properly</code></div><div><code>* implement the XMLHttpRequest in IE7 (can't request local files),</code></div><div><code>* so we use the ActiveXObject when it is available</code></div><div><code>* Additionally XMLHttpRequest can be disabled in IE7/IE8 so</code></div><div><code>* we need a fallback.</code></div><div><code>*/</code></div><div><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>!</code><code>this</code><code>.isLocal && createStandardXHR() || createActiveXHR();</code></div><div><code>} :</code></div><div><code>// For all other browsers, use the standard XMLHttpRequest object</code></div><div><code>createStandardXHR;</code></div><div><code>...</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Whilst the following block of code is also a level above the actual jQuery XHR (<code>jqXHR</code>) implementation, it's the convenience facade that we actually most commonly interact with:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_904403"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Request the remote document</code></div><div><code>jQuery.ajax({</code></div><div><code>url: url,</code></div><div><code>type: type,</code></div><div><code>dataType: </code><code>"html"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>data: params,</code></div><div><code>// Complete callback (responseText is used internally)</code></div><div><code>complete: </code><code>function</code><code>( jqXHR, status, responseText ) {</code></div><div><code>// Store the response as specified by the jqXHR object</code></div><div><code>responseText = jqXHR.responseText;</code></div><div><code>// If successful, inject the HTML into all the matched elements</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( jqXHR.isResolved() ) {</code></div><div><code>// Get the actual response in case</code></div><div><code>// a dataFilter is present in ajaxSettings</code></div><div><code>jqXHR.done(</code><code>function</code><code>( r ) {</code></div><div><code>responseText = r;</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>// See if a selector was specified</code></div><div><code>self.html( selector ?</code></div><div><code>// Create a dummy div to hold the results</code></div><div><code>jQuery(</code><code>"&lt;div&gt;"</code><code>)</code></div><div><code>// inject the contents of the document in, removing the scripts</code></div><div><code>// to avoid any 'Permission Denied' errors in IE</code></div><div><code>.append(responseText.replace(rscript, </code><code>""</code><code>))</code></div><div><code>// Locate the specified elements</code></div><div><code>.find(selector) :</code></div><div><code>// If not, just inject the full result</code></div><div><code>responseText );</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( callback ) {</code></div><div><code>self.each( callback, [ responseText, status, jqXHR ] );</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>&lt;/div&gt;</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<h2 id="observerpatternjquery">
  <a href="#observerpatternjquery" target="_blank">#</a>
  The Observer Pattern
</h2>
<p>
  Another pattern we reviewed earlier is the Observer (Publish/Subscribe) pattern. This is where the objects in a system may subscribe to other objects and be notified by them when an event of interest occurs.
</p>
<p>
jQuery core has come with built-in support for a publish/subscribe-like system for a few years now, which it refers to as <em>custom events</em>.</p>

<p>In earlier versions of the library, access to these custom events was possible using <code>jQuery.bind()</code> (subscribe), <code>jQuery.trigger()</code> (publish) and <code>jQuery.unbind()</code> (unsubscribe), but in recent versions this can be done using <code>jQuery.on()</code>, <code>jQuery.trigger()</code> and <code>jQuery.off()</code>.</p>
<p>
  Below we can see an example of this being used in practice:
</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_159133"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Equivalent to subscribe(topicName, callback)</code></div><div><code>$( document ).on( </code><code>"topicName"</code><code>, </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>//..perform some behaviour</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>// Equivalent to publish(topicName)</code></div><div><code>$( document ).trigger( </code><code>"topicName"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// Equivalent to unsubscribe(topicName)</code></div><div><code>$( document ).off( </code><code>"topicName"</code> <code>);</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>Calls to <code>jQuery.on()</code> and <code>jQuery.off()</code> eventually go through the jQuery events system. Similar to Ajax, as the implementation for this is relatively long, we can instead look at where and how the actual event handlers for custom events are attached:</p>


<div><div id="highlighter_252075"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>jQuery.event = {</code></div><div><code>add: </code><code>function</code><code>( elem, types, handler, data, selector ) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>elemData, eventHandle, events,</code></div><div><code>t, tns, type, namespaces, handleObj,</code></div><div><code>handleObjIn, quick, handlers, special;</code></div><div><code>...</code></div><div><code>// Init the element's event structure and main handler,</code></div><div><code>//if this is the first</code></div><div><code>events = elemData.events;</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( !events ) {</code></div><div><code>elemData.events = events = {};</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>...</code></div><div><code>// Handle multiple events separated by a space</code></div><div><code>// jQuery(...).bind("mouseover mouseout", fn);</code></div><div><code>types = jQuery.trim( hoverHack(types) ).split( </code><code>" "</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>for</code> <code>( t = 0; t &lt; types.length; t++ ) {</code></div><div><code>...</code></div><div><code>// Init the event handler queue if we're the first</code></div><div><code>handlers = events[ type ];</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( !handlers ) {</code></div><div><code>handlers = events[ type ] = [];</code></div><div><code>handlers.delegateCount = 0;</code></div><div><code>// Only use addEventListener/attachEvent if the special</code></div><div><code>// events handler returns false</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( !special.setup || special.setup.call( elem, data,</code></div><div><code>//namespaces, eventHandle ) === false ) {</code></div><div><code>// Bind the global event handler to the element</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( elem.addEventListener ) {</code></div><div><code>elem.addEventListener( type, eventHandle, </code><code>false</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>else</code> <code>if</code> <code>( elem.attachEvent ) {</code></div><div><code>elem.attachEvent( </code><code>"on"</code> <code>+ type, eventHandle );</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>For those that prefer to use the conventional naming scheme for the Observer pattern, <a href="https://gist.github.com/661855" target="_blank">Ben Alman</a> created a simple wrapper around the above methods which provides us access to <code>jQuery.publish()</code>, <code>jQuery.subscribe</code>, and <code>jQuery.unsubscribe</code> methods. I've previously linked to them earlier in the book, but we can see the wrapper in full below.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_954373"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>(</code><code>function</code><code>( $ ) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>o = $({});</code></div><div><code>$.subscribe = </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>o.on.apply(o, arguments);</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>$.unsubscribe = </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>o.off.apply(o, arguments);</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>$.publish = </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>o.trigger.apply(o, arguments);</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>}( jQuery ));</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>In recent versions of jQuery, a multi-purpose callbacks object (<code>jQuery.Callbacks</code>) was made available to enable users to write new solutions based on callback lists. One such solution to write using this feature is another Publish/Subscribe system. An implementation of this is the following:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_724825"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>topics = {};</code></div><div><code>jQuery.Topic = </code><code>function</code><code>( id ) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>callbacks,</code></div><div><code>topic = id && topics[ id ];</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( !topic ) {</code></div><div><code>callbacks = jQuery.Callbacks();</code></div><div><code>topic = {</code></div><div><code>publish: callbacks.fire,</code></div><div><code>subscribe: callbacks.add,</code></div><div><code>unsubscribe: callbacks.remove</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( id ) {</code></div><div><code>topics[ id ] = topic;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>topic;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

which can then be used as follows:
<div><div id="highlighter_100350"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Subscribers</code></div><div><code>$.Topic( </code><code>"mailArrived"</code> <code>).subscribe( fn1 );</code></div><div><code>$.Topic( </code><code>"mailArrived"</code> <code>).subscribe( fn2 );</code></div><div><code>$.Topic( </code><code>"mailSent"</code> <code>).subscribe( fn1 );</code></div><div><code>// Publisher</code></div><div><code>$.Topic( </code><code>"mailArrived"</code> <code>).publish( </code><code>"hello world!"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>$.Topic( </code><code>"mailSent"</code> <code>).publish( </code><code>"woo! mail!"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// Here, "hello world!" gets pushed to fn1 and fn2</code></div><div><code>// when the "mailArrived" notification is published</code></div><div><code>// with "woo! mail!" also being pushed to fn1 when</code></div><div><code>// the "mailSent" notification is published.</code></div><div><code>// Outputs:</code></div><div><code>// hello world!</code></div><div><code>// fn2 says: hello world!</code></div><div><code>// woo! mail!</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<h2 id="iteratorpatternjquery">
  <a href="#iteratorpatternjquery" target="_blank">#</a>
  The Iterator Pattern
</h2>
<p>
  The Iterator is a design pattern where iterators (objects that allow us to traverse through all the elements of a collection) access the elements of an aggregate object sequentially without needing to expose its underlying form.</p>

 <p>Iterators encapsulate the internal structure of how that particular iteration occurs. In the case of jQuery's <code>jQuery.fn.each()</code> iterator, we are actually able to use the underlying code behind <code>jQuery.each()</code> to iterate through a collection, without needing to see or understand the code working behind the scenes providing this capability.</p>

<p>This is a pattern that could be considered a special case of the facade, where we explicitly deal with problems related to iteration.</p>


<div><div id="highlighter_144975"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$.each( [</code><code>"john"</code><code>,</code><code>"dave"</code><code>,</code><code>"rick"</code><code>,</code><code>"julian"</code><code>], </code><code>function</code><code>( index, value ) {</code></div><div><code>console.log( index + </code><code>": "</code> <code>+ value);</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>$( </code><code>"li"</code> <code>).each( </code><code>function</code> <code>( index ) {</code></div><div><code>console.log( index + </code><code>": "</code> <code>+ $( </code><code>this</code> <code>).text());</code></div><div><code>});</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>


<p>Here we can see the code for <code>jQuery.fn.each()</code>:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_760440"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Execute a callback for every element in the matched set.</code></div><div><code>each: </code><code>function</code><code>( callback, args ) {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>jQuery.each( </code><code>this</code><code>, callback, args );</code></div><div><code>}</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Followed by the code behind <code>jQuery.each()</code> which handles two ways of iterating through objects:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_144952"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>each: </code><code>function</code><code>( object, callback, args ) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>name, i = 0,</code></div><div><code>length = object.length,</code></div><div><code>isObj = length === undefined || jQuery.isFunction( object );</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( args ) {</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( isObj ) {</code></div><div><code>for</code> <code>( name </code><code>in</code> <code>object ) {</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( callback.apply( object[ name ], args ) === </code><code>false</code> <code>) {</code></div><div><code>break</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>else</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>for</code> <code>( ; i &lt; length; ) {</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( callback.apply( object[ i++ ], args ) === </code><code>false</code> <code>) {</code></div><div><code>break</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// A special, fast, case for the most common use of each</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>else</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( isObj ) {</code></div><div><code>for</code> <code>( name </code><code>in</code> <code>object ) {</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( callback.call( object[ name ], name, object[ name ] ) === </code><code>false</code> <code>) {</code></div><div><code>break</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>else</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>for</code> <code>( ; i &lt; length; ) {</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( callback.call( object[ i ], i, object[ i++ ] ) === </code><code>false</code> <code>) {</code></div><div><code>break</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>object;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<h2 id="lazyinitialisationjquery">The Lazy Initialization Pattern</h2>
<p>
  <strong>Lazy Initialization </strong>is a design pattern which allows us to delay expensive processes until the first instance they are needed. An example of this is the <code>.ready()</code> function in jQuery that only executes a callback once the DOM is ready.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_763520"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$( document ).ready( </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>// The ajax request won't attempt to execute until</code></div><div><code>// the DOM is ready</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>jqxhr = $.ajax({</code></div><div><code>url: </code><code>"<a href="http://domain.com/api/" target="_blank">http://domain.com/api/</a>"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>data: </code><code>"display=latest&order=ascending"</code></div><div><code>})</code></div><div><code>.done( </code><code>function</code><code>( data ) ){</code></div><div><code>$(</code><code>".status"</code><code>).html( </code><code>"content loaded"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"Data output:"</code> <code>+ data );</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>});</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p><code>jQuery.fn.ready()</code> is powered by <code>jQuery.bindReady()</code>, seen below:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_802157"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>bindReady: </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( readyList ) {</code></div><div><code>return</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>readyList = jQuery.Callbacks( </code><code>"once memory"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// Catch cases where $(document).ready() is called after the</code></div><div><code>// browser event has already occurred.</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( document.readyState === </code><code>"complete"</code> <code>) {</code></div><div><code>// Handle it asynchronously to allow scripts the opportunity to delay ready</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>setTimeout( jQuery.ready, 1 );</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Mozilla, Opera and webkit support this event</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( document.addEventListener ) {</code></div><div><code>// Use the handy event callback</code></div><div><code>document.addEventListener( </code><code>"DOMContentLoaded"</code><code>, DOMContentLoaded, </code><code>false</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// A fallback to window.onload, that will always work</code></div><div><code>window.addEventListener( </code><code>"load"</code><code>, jQuery.ready, </code><code>false</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// If IE event model is used</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>else</code> <code>if</code> <code>( document.attachEvent ) {</code></div><div><code>// ensure firing before onload,</code></div><div><code>// maybe late but safe also for iframes</code></div><div><code>document.attachEvent( </code><code>"onreadystatechange"</code><code>, DOMContentLoaded );</code></div><div><code>// A fallback to window.onload, that will always work</code></div><div><code>window.attachEvent( </code><code>"onload"</code><code>, jQuery.ready );</code></div><div><code>// If IE and not a frame</code></div><div><code>// continually check to see if the document is ready</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>toplevel = </code><code>false</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>try</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>toplevel = window.frameElement == </code><code>null</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>catch</code><code>(e) {}</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( document.documentElement.doScroll && toplevel ) {</code></div><div><code>doScrollCheck();</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>},</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Whilst not directly used in jQuery core, some developers may also be familiar with the concept of LazyLoading via plugins such as <a href="http://www.appelsiini.net/projects/lazyload" target="_blank">this</a>.</p>

<p>LazyLoading is effectively the same as Lazy initialization and is a technique whereby additional data on a page is loaded when needed (e.g. when a user has scrolled to the end of the page). In recent years this pattern has become quite prominent and can be currently be found in both the Twitter and Facebook UIs.</p>

<h2 id="proxypatternjquery">
  <a href="#proxypatternjquery" target="_blank">#</a>
  The Proxy Pattern
</h2>

<p>There are times when it is necessary for us to control the access and context behind an object and this is where the Proxy pattern can be useful.</p>

<p>It can help us control when an expensive object should be instantiated, provide advanced ways to reference the object or modify the object to function a particular way in specific contexts. </p>

<p>In jQuery core, a <code>jQuery.proxy()</code> method exists which accepts as input a function and returns a new one which will always have a specific context. This ensures that the value of <code>this</code> within a function is the value we expect.</p>

<p>An example of where this is useful is when we're making use of timers within a <code>click</code> event handler. Imagine we have the following handler prior to adding any timers:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_269269"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$( </code><code>"button"</code> <code>).on( </code><code>"click"</code><code>, </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>// Within this function, "this" refers to the element that was clicked</code></div><div><code>$( </code><code>this</code> <code>).addClass( </code><code>"active"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>});</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>If we wished to add a hard delay before the <code>active</code> class was added, we <em>could</em> use <code>setTimeout()</code> to achieve this. Unfortunately there is a small problem with this solution: whatever function is passed to <code>setTimeout()</code> will have a different value for <code>this</code> within that function. It will instead refer to the <code>window</code> object, which is not what we desire.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_326218"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$( </code><code>"button"</code> <code>).on( </code><code>"click"</code><code>, </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>setTimeout(</code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>// "this" doesn't refer to our element!</code></div><div><code>// It refers to window</code></div><div><code>$( </code><code>this</code> <code>).addClass( </code><code>"active"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>});</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>To work around this problem, we can use <code>jQuery.proxy()</code> to implement a type of proxy pattern. By calling it with the function and value we would like assigned to <code>this</code> it will actually return a function that retains the value we desire within the correct context. Here's how this would look:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_241666"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$( </code><code>"button"</code> <code>).on( </code><code>"click"</code><code>, </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>setTimeout( $.proxy( </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>// "this" now refers to our element as we wanted</code></div><div><code>$( </code><code>this</code> <code>).addClass( </code><code>"active"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>}, </code><code>this</code><code>), 500);</code></div><div><code>// the last "this" we're passing tells $.proxy() that our DOM element</code></div><div><code>// is the value we want "this" to refer to.</code></div><div><code>});</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>jQuery's implementation of <code>jQuery.proxy()</code> can be found below:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_18864"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// Bind a function to a context, optionally partially applying any</code></div><div><code>// arguments.</code></div><div><code>proxy: </code><code>function</code><code>( fn, context ) {</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( </code><code>typeof</code> <code>context === </code><code>"string"</code> <code>) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>tmp = fn[ context ];</code></div><div><code>context = fn;</code></div><div><code>fn = tmp;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Quick check to determine if target is callable, in the spec</code></div><div><code>// this throws a TypeError, but we will just return undefined.</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( !jQuery.isFunction( fn ) ) {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>undefined;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Simulated bind</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>args = slice.call( arguments, 2 ),</code></div><div><code>proxy = </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>fn.apply( context, args.concat( slice.call( arguments ) ) );</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// Set the guid of unique handler to the same of original handler, so it can be removed</code></div><div><code>proxy.guid = fn.guid = fn.guid || proxy.guid || jQuery.guid++;</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>proxy;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>



<h2 id="builderpatternjquery">
  <a href="#builderpatternjquery" target="_blank">#</a>
  The Builder Pattern
</h2>

<p>When working with the DOM, we often want to construct new elements dynamically - a process which can increase in complexity depending on the final markup, attributes and properties we wish our constructed elements to contain.</p>

<p>Complex elements require special care when being defined, especially if we want the flexibility to either literally define the final markup for our elements (which can get messy) or take a more readable object-oriented route instead. Having a mechanism for building our complex DOM objects that is independent from the objects themselves gives us this flexibility and that is exactly what the Builder pattern provides.</p>

<p>Builders allow us to construct complex objects by only specifying the type and content of the object, shielding us from the process of creating or representing the object explicitly.</p>

<p>The jQuery dollar sign allows us to do just this as it provides a number of different means for dynamically building new jQuery (and DOM) objects, by either passing in the complete markup for an element, partial markup and content or using the jQuery for construction:</p>


<div><div id="highlighter_478401"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$( </code><code>'&lt;div class="foo"&gt;bar&lt;/div&gt;'</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>$( </code><code>'&lt;p id="test"&gt;foo &lt;em&gt;bar&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;'</code><code>).appendTo(</code><code>"body"</code><code>);</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>newParagraph = $( </code><code>"&lt;p /&gt;"</code> <code>).text( </code><code>"Hello world"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>$( </code><code>"&lt;input /&gt;"</code> <code>)</code></div><div><code>.attr({ </code><code>"type"</code><code>: </code><code>"text"</code><code>, </code><code>"id"</code><code>:</code><code>"sample"</code><code>})</code></div><div><code>.appendTo(</code><code>"#container"</code><code>);</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>Below is a snippet from jQuery core's internal <code>jQuery.prototype</code> method which assists with the construction of jQuery objects from markup passed to the <code>jQuery()</code> selector. Regardless of whether or not <code>document.createElement</code> is used to create a new element, a reference to the element (found or created) is injected into the returned object so further methods such as <code>.attr()</code> can be easily used on it right after.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_85656"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// HANDLE: $(html) -&gt; $(array)</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( match[1] ) {</code></div><div><code>context = context </code><code>instanceof</code> <code>jQuery ? context[0] : context;</code></div><div><code>doc = ( context ? context.ownerDocument || context : document );</code></div><div><code>// If a single string is passed in and it's a single tag</code></div><div><code>// just do a createElement and skip the rest</code></div><div><code>ret = rsingleTag.exec( selector );</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( ret ) {</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( jQuery.isPlainObject( context ) ) {</code></div><div><code>selector = [ document.createElement( ret[1] ) ];</code></div><div><code>jQuery.fn.attr.call( selector, context, </code><code>true</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>else</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>selector = [ doc.createElement( ret[1] ) ];</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>else</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>ret = jQuery.buildFragment( [ match[1] ], [ doc ] );</code></div><div><code>selector = ( ret.cacheable ? jQuery.clone(ret.fragment) : ret.fragment ).childNodes;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>jQuery.merge( </code><code>this</code><code>, selector );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>



<h1 id="jquerypluginpatterns">
  <a href="#jquerypluginpatterns" target="_blank">#</a>
  jQuery Plugin Design Patterns
</h1>


<p>jQuery plugin development has evolved over the past few years. We no longer have just one way to write plugins, but many. In reality, certain plugin design patterns might work better for a particular problem or component than others.</p>

<p>Some developers may wish to use the jQuery UI <a href="http://ajpiano.com/widgetfactory/#slide1" target="_blank">widget factory</a>; it’s great for complex, flexible UI components. Some may not.</p>

<p>Some might like to structure their plugins more like modules (similar to the module pattern) or use a more modern module format such as AMD.</p>

<p>Some might want their plugins to harness the power of prototypal inheritance. Others may wish to use custom events or Publish/Subscribe to communicate from plugins to the rest of their app. And so on.</p>

<p>I began to think about plugin patterns after noticing a number of efforts to create a one-size-fits-all jQuery plugin boilerplate. While such a boilerplate is a great idea in theory, the reality is that we rarely write plugins in one fixed way, using a single pattern all the time.</p>

<p>Let us assume that we’ve tried our hand at writing our own jQuery plugins at some point and we’re comfortable putting together something that works. It’s functional. It does what it needs to do, but perhaps we feel it could be structured better. Maybe it could be more flexible or could be designed to address more of the issues developers commonly run into. If this sounds familiar, then you might find this chapter useful. In it, we're going to explore a number of jQuery plugin patterns that have worked well for other developers in the wild.</p>


<p><strong>Note:</strong> This chapter is targeted at intermediate to advanced developers, although we will briefly review some jQuery plugin fundamentals to begin.

</p><p>If you don’t feel quite ready for this just yet, I’m happy to recommend the official jQuery <a href="http://docs.jquery.com/Plugins/Authoring" target="_blank">Plugins/Authoring</a> guide, Ben Alman’s <a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/scriptjunkie/ff696759" target="_blank">plugin style guide</a> and Remy Sharp’s “<a href="http://remysharp.com/2010/06/03/signs-of-a-poorly-written-jquery-plugin/" target="_blank">Signs of a Poorly Written jQuery Plugin</a>.” as reading material prior to starting this section.</p>

<h3>Patterns</h3>

<p>jQuery plugins have few concrete rules, which is one of the reasons for the incredible diversity in how they are implemented across the community. At the most basic level, we can write a plugin simply by adding a new function property to jQuery’s <code>jQuery.fn</code> object, as follows:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_34065"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$.fn.myPluginName = </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>// our plugin logic</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>This is great for compactness, but the following would be a better foundation to build on:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_810711"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>(</code><code>function</code><code>( $ ){</code></div><div><code>$.fn.myPluginName = </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>// our plugin logic</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>})( jQuery );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Here, we’ve wrapped our plugin logic in an anonymous function. To ensure that our use of the <code>$</code> sign as a shorthand creates no conflicts between jQuery and other JavaScript libraries, we simply pass it to this closure, which maps it to the dollar sign. This ensures that it can’t be affected by anything outside of its scope of execution.</p>

<p>An alternative way to write this pattern would be to use <code>jQuery.extend()</code>, which enables us to define multiple functions at once and which sometimes make more sense semantically:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_87128"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>(</code><code>function</code><code>( $ ){</code></div><div><code>$.extend($.fn, {</code></div><div><code>myplugin: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>// your plugin logic</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>})( jQuery );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>We have now reviewed some jQuery plugin fundamentals, but a lot more could be done to take this further. <em>A Lightweight Start</em> is the first complete plugin design pattern we’ll be exploring and it covers some best practices that we can use for basic everyday plugin development, taking into account common gotchas worth applying.</p>



<p>While most of the patterns below will be explained, I recommend reading through the comments in the code, because they will offer more insight into why certain best practices are applied. </p>

<p>I should also mention that none of this would be possible without the previous work, input and advice of other members of the jQuery community. I’ve listed them inline with each pattern so that one can read up on their individual work if interested.</p>

<h3>'A Lightweight Start' Pattern</h3>

<p>Let’s begin our deeper look at plugin patterns with something basic that follows best practices (including those in the jQuery plugin-authoring guide). This pattern is ideal for developers who are either new to plugin development or who just want to achieve something simple (such as a utility plugin). <em>A Lightweight Start</em> uses the following:</p>

<ul>
<li>Common best practices such as a semi-colon placed before the functions invocation (we'll go through why in the comments below)</li>

<li><code>window, document, undefined</code> passed in as arguments.</li>

<li>A basic defaults object.</li>

<li>A simple plugin constructor for logic related to the initial creation and the assignment of the element to work with.</li>

<li>Extending the options with defaults.</li>

<li>A lightweight wrapper around the constructor, which helps to avoid issues such as multiple instantiations.</li>

<li>Adherence to the jQuery core style guidelines for maximized readability.</li>
</ul>

<div><div id="highlighter_105771"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>/*!</code></div><div><code>* jQuery lightweight plugin boilerplate</code></div><div><code>* Original author: @ajpiano</code></div><div><code>* Further changes, comments: @addyosmani</code></div><div><code>* Licensed under the MIT license</code></div><div><code>*/</code></div><div><code>// the semi-colon before the function invocation is a safety</code></div><div><code>// net against concatenated scripts and/or other plugins</code></div><div><code>// that are not closed properly.</code></div><div><code>;(</code><code>function</code> <code>( $, window, document, undefined ) {</code></div><div><code>// undefined is used here as the undefined global</code></div><div><code>// variable in ECMAScript 3 and is mutable (i.e. it can</code></div><div><code>// be changed by someone else). undefined isn't really</code></div><div><code>// being passed in so we can ensure that its value is</code></div><div><code>// truly undefined. In ES5, undefined can no longer be</code></div><div><code>// modified.</code></div><div><code>// window and document are passed through as local</code></div><div><code>// variables rather than as globals, because this (slightly)</code></div><div><code>// quickens the resolution process and can be more</code></div><div><code>// efficiently minified (especially when both are</code></div><div><code>// regularly referenced in our plugin).</code></div><div><code>// Create the defaults once</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>pluginName = </code><code>"defaultPluginName"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>defaults = {</code></div><div><code>propertyName: </code><code>"value"</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// The actual plugin constructor</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>Plugin( element, options ) {</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.element = element;</code></div><div><code>// jQuery has an extend method that merges the</code></div><div><code>// contents of two or more objects, storing the</code></div><div><code>// result in the first object. The first object</code></div><div><code>// is generally empty because we don't want to alter</code></div><div><code>// the default options for future instances of the plugin</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.options = $.extend( {}, defaults, options) ;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>._defaults = defaults;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>._name = pluginName;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.init();</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>Plugin.prototype.init = </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>// Place initialization logic here</code></div><div><code>// We already have access to the DOM element and</code></div><div><code>// the options via the instance, e.g. this.element</code></div><div><code>// and this.options</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// A really lightweight plugin wrapper around the constructor,</code></div><div><code>// preventing against multiple instantiations</code></div><div><code>$.fn[pluginName] = </code><code>function</code> <code>( options ) {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.each(</code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( !$.data(</code><code>this</code><code>, </code><code>"plugin_"</code> <code>+ pluginName )) {</code></div><div><code>$.data( </code><code>this</code><code>, </code><code>"plugin_"</code> <code>+ pluginName,</code></div><div><code>new</code> <code>Plugin( </code><code>this</code><code>, options ));</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>})( jQuery, window, document );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Usage:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_867631"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$(</code><code>"#elem"</code><code>).defaultPluginName({</code></div><div><code>propertyName: </code><code>"a custom value"</code></div><div><code>});</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><h4>Further Reading</h4>



<h3>“Complete” Widget Factory Pattern</h3>

<p>While the jQuery plugin authoring guide is a great introduction to plugin development, it doesn't help obscure away common plugin plumbing tasks that we have to deal with on a regular basis.</p>

<p>The jQuery UI Widget Factory is a solution to this problem that helps us build complex, stateful plugins based on object-oriented principles. It also eases communication with our plugins instance, obfuscating a number of the repetitive tasks that we would have to code when working with basic plugins.</p>

<p>Stateful plugins help us keep track of their current state, also allowing us to change properties of the plugin after it has been initialized.</p>

<p>One of the great things about the Widget Factory is that the majority of the jQuery UI library actually uses it as a base for its components. This means that if we’re looking for further guidance on structure beyond this pattern, we won’t have to look beyond the jQuery UI repository on GitHub (<a href="https://github.com/jquery/jquery-ui" target="_blank">https://github.com/jquery/jquery-ui</a>).</p>

<p>This jQuery UI Widget Factory pattern covers almost all of the supported default factory methods, including triggering events. As per the last pattern, comments are included for all of the methods used and further guidance is given in the inline comments.</p>


<div><div id="highlighter_18243"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>/*!</code></div><div><code>* jQuery UI Widget-factory plugin boilerplate (for 1.8/9+)</code></div><div><code>* Author: @addyosmani</code></div><div><code>* Further changes: @peolanha</code></div><div><code>* Licensed under the MIT license</code></div><div><code>*/</code></div><div><code>;(</code><code>function</code> <code>( $, window, document, undefined ) {</code></div><div><code>// define our widget under a namespace of your choice</code></div><div><code>// with additional parameters e.g.</code></div><div><code>// $.widget( "namespace.widgetname", (optional) - an</code></div><div><code>// existing widget prototype to inherit from, an object</code></div><div><code>// literal to become the widget's prototype );</code></div><div><code>$.widget( </code><code>"namespace.widgetname"</code><code>, {</code></div><div><code>//Options to be used as defaults</code></div><div><code>options: {</code></div><div><code>someValue: </code><code>null</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>//Setup widget (e.g. element creation, apply theming</code></div><div><code>//, bind events etc.)</code></div><div><code>_create: </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>// _create will automatically run the first time</code></div><div><code>// this widget is called. Put the initial widget</code></div><div><code>// setup code here, then we can access the element</code></div><div><code>// on which the widget was called via this.element.</code></div><div><code>// The options defined above can be accessed</code></div><div><code>// via this.options this.element.addStuff();</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>// Destroy an instantiated plugin and clean up</code></div><div><code>// modifications the widget has made to the DOM</code></div><div><code>destroy: </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>// this.element.removeStuff();</code></div><div><code>// For UI 1.8, destroy must be invoked from the</code></div><div><code>// base widget</code></div><div><code>$.Widget.prototype.destroy.call( </code><code>this</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// For UI 1.9, define _destroy instead and don't</code></div><div><code>// worry about</code></div><div><code>// calling the base widget</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>methodB: </code><code>function</code> <code>( event ) {</code></div><div><code>//_trigger dispatches callbacks the plugin user</code></div><div><code>// can subscribe to</code></div><div><code>// signature: _trigger( "callbackName", [eventObject],</code></div><div><code>// [uiObject] )</code></div><div><code>// e.g. this._trigger( "hover", e /*where e.type ==</code></div><div><code>// "mouseenter"*/, { hovered: $(e.target)});</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>._trigger( </code><code>"methodA"</code><code>, event, {</code></div><div><code>key: value</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>methodA: </code><code>function</code> <code>( event ) {</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>._trigger( </code><code>"dataChanged"</code><code>, event, {</code></div><div><code>key: value</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>// Respond to any changes the user makes to the</code></div><div><code>// option method</code></div><div><code>_setOption: </code><code>function</code> <code>( key, value ) {</code></div><div><code>switch</code> <code>( key ) {</code></div><div><code>case</code> <code>"someValue"</code><code>:</code></div><div><code>// this.options.someValue = doSomethingWith( value );</code></div><div><code>break</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>default</code><code>:</code></div><div><code>// this.options[ key ] = value;</code></div><div><code>break</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// For UI 1.8, _setOption must be manually invoked</code></div><div><code>// from the base widget</code></div><div><code>$.Widget.prototype._setOption.apply( </code><code>this</code><code>, arguments );</code></div><div><code>// For UI 1.9 the _super method can be used instead</code></div><div><code>// this._super( "_setOption", key, value );</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>})( jQuery, window, document );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Usage:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_805136"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>collection = $(</code><code>"#elem"</code><code>).widgetName({</code></div><div><code>foo: </code><code>false</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>collection.widgetName(</code><code>"methodB"</code><code>);</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><h4>Further Reading</h4>



<h3>Nested Namespacing Plugin Pattern</h3>

<p>As we've previously covered in the book, namespacing our code is a way to avoid collisions with other objects and variables in the global namespace. They’re important because we want to safeguard our plugin from breaking in the event that another script on the page uses the same variable or plugin names as ours. As a good citizen of the global namespace, we must also do our best not to prevent other developers scripts from executing because of the same issues.</p>

<p>JavaScript doesn't really have built-in support for namespaces as other languages do, but it does have objects that can be used to achieve a similar effect. Employing a top-level object as the name of our namespace, we can easily check for the existence of another object on the page with the same name. If such an object does not exist, then we define it; if it does exist, then we simply extend it with our plugin.</p>

<p>Objects (or, rather, object literals) can be used to create nested namespaces, such as <code>namespace.subnamespace.pluginName</code> and so on. But to keep things simple, the namespacing boilerplate below should show us everything we need to get started with these concepts.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_923695"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>/*!</code></div><div><code>* jQuery namespaced "Starter" plugin boilerplate</code></div><div><code>* Author: @dougneiner</code></div><div><code>* Further changes: @addyosmani</code></div><div><code>* Licensed under the MIT license</code></div><div><code>*/</code></div><div><code>;(</code><code>function</code> <code>( $ ) {</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>(!$.myNamespace) {</code></div><div><code>$.myNamespace = {};</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>$.myNamespace.myPluginName = </code><code>function</code> <code>( el, myFunctionParam, options ) {</code></div><div><code>// To avoid scope issues, use "base" instead of "this"</code></div><div><code>// to reference this class from internal events and functions.</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>base = </code><code>this</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>// Access to jQuery and DOM versions of element</code></div><div><code>base.$el = $( el );</code></div><div><code>base.el = el;</code></div><div><code>// Add a reverse reference to the DOM object</code></div><div><code>base.$el.data( </code><code>"myNamespace.myPluginName"</code><code>, base );</code></div><div><code>base.init = </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>base.myFunctionParam = myFunctionParam;</code></div><div><code>base.options = $.extend({},</code></div><div><code>$.myNamespace.myPluginName.defaultOptions, options);</code></div><div><code>// Put our initialization code here</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// Sample Function, Uncomment to use</code></div><div><code>// base.functionName = function( parameters ){</code></div><div><code>//</code></div><div><code>// };</code></div><div><code>// Run initializer</code></div><div><code>base.init();</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>$.myNamespace.myPluginName.defaultOptions = {</code></div><div><code>myDefaultValue: </code><code>""</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>$.fn.mynamespace_myPluginName = </code><code>function</code></div><div><code>( myFunctionParam, options ) {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.each(</code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>(</code><code>new</code> <code>$.myNamespace.myPluginName( </code><code>this</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>myFunctionParam, options ));</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>})( jQuery );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Usage:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_222087"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$(</code><code>"#elem"</code><code>).mynamespace_myPluginName({</code></div><div><code>myDefaultValue: </code><code>"foobar"</code></div><div><code>});</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><h4>Further Reading</h4>



<h3>Custom Events Plugin Pattern (With The Widget factory)</h3>

<p>In the <em>JavaScript Design Patterns</em> section of the book, we discussed the Observer pattern and later went on to cover jQuery's support for custom events, which offer a similar solution for implementing Publish/Subscribe. This same pattern can be used when writing jQuery plugins.</p>

<p>The basic idea here is that objects in a page can publish event notifications when something interesting occurs in our application. Other objects then subscribe to (or listen) for these events and respond accordingly. This results in the logic for our application being significantly more decoupled, as each object no longer needs to directly communicate with another.</p>

<p>In the following jQuery UI widget factory pattern, we’ll implement a basic custom event-based Publish/Subscribe system that allows our plugin to subscribe to event notifications from the rest of our application, which will be responsible for publishing them.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_724762"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>/*!</code></div><div><code>* jQuery custom-events plugin boilerplate</code></div><div><code>* Author: DevPatch</code></div><div><code>* Further changes: @addyosmani</code></div><div><code>* Licensed under the MIT license</code></div><div><code>*/</code></div><div><code>// In this pattern, we use jQuery's custom events to add</code></div><div><code>// pub/sub (publish/subscribe) capabilities to widgets.</code></div><div><code>// Each widget would publish certain events and subscribe</code></div><div><code>// to others. This approach effectively helps to decouple</code></div><div><code>// the widgets and enables them to function independently.</code></div><div><code>;(</code><code>function</code> <code>( $, window, document, undefined ) {</code></div><div><code>$.widget( </code><code>"ao.eventStatus"</code><code>, {</code></div><div><code>options: {</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>_create: </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>self = </code><code>this</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>//self.element.addClass( "my-widget" );</code></div><div><code>//subscribe to "myEventStart"</code></div><div><code>self.element.on( </code><code>"myEventStart"</code><code>, </code><code>function</code><code>( e ) {</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"event start"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>//subscribe to "myEventEnd"</code></div><div><code>self.element.on( </code><code>"myEventEnd"</code><code>, </code><code>function</code><code>( e ) {</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"event end"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>//unsubscribe to "myEventStart"</code></div><div><code>//self.element.off( "myEventStart", function(e){</code></div><div><code>///console.log( "unsubscribed to this event" );</code></div><div><code>//});</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>destroy: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>$.Widget.prototype.destroy.apply( </code><code>this</code><code>, arguments );</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>})( jQuery, window, document );</code></div><div><code>// Publishing event notifications</code></div><div><code>// $( ".my-widget" ).trigger( "myEventStart");</code></div><div><code>// $( ".my-widget" ).trigger( "myEventEnd" );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Usage:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_548980"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>el = $( </code><code>"#elem"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>el.eventStatus();</code></div><div><code>el.eventStatus().trigger( </code><code>"myEventStart"</code> <code>);</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><h4>Further Reading</h4>

<ul>
<li>“<a href="http://www.devpatch.com/articles/2010-03-22-communication-between-jquery-ui-widgets/" target="_blank">Communication Between jQuery UI Widgets</a>,” Benjamin Sternthal</li>
</ul>

<h3>Prototypal Inheritance With The DOM-To-Object Bridge Pattern</h3>

<p>As covered earlier, in JavaScript, we don’t have the traditional notion of classes that we would find in other classical programming languages, but we do have prototypal inheritance. With prototypal inheritance, an object inherits from another object. We can apply this concept to jQuery plugin development.</p>

<p>Yepnope.js author <a href="http://alexsexton.com/" target="_blank">Alex Sexton</a> and jQuery team member <a href="http://scottgonzalez.com/" target="_blank">Scott Gonzalez</a> have looked at this topic in detail. In sum, they discovered that for organized modular development, clearly separating the object that defines the logic for a plugin from the plugin-generation process itself can be beneficial.</p>

<p>The benefit is that testing our plugins code becomes significantly easier and we are also able to adjust the way things work behind the scenes without altering the way that any object APIs we implement are used.</p>

<p>In Sexton’s article on this topic, he implemented a bridge that enables us to attach our general logic to a particular plugin, which we’ve implemented in the pattern below.</p>

<p>One of the other advantages of this pattern is that we don’t have to constantly repeat the same plugin initialization code, thus ensuring that the concepts behind DRY development are maintained. Some developers might also find this pattern easier to read than others.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_111309"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>/*!</code></div><div><code>* jQuery prototypal inheritance plugin boilerplate</code></div><div><code>* Author: Alex Sexton, Scott Gonzalez</code></div><div><code>* Further changes: @addyosmani</code></div><div><code>* Licensed under the MIT license</code></div><div><code>*/</code></div><div><code>// myObject - an object representing a concept we wish to model</code></div><div><code>// (e.g. a car)</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>myObject = {</code></div><div><code>init: </code><code>function</code><code>( options, elem ) {</code></div><div><code>// Mix in the passed-in options with the default options</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.options = $.extend( {}, </code><code>this</code><code>.options, options );</code></div><div><code>// Save the element reference, both as a jQuery</code></div><div><code>// reference and a normal reference</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.elem = elem;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.$elem = $( elem );</code></div><div><code>// Build the DOM's initial structure</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>._build();</code></div><div><code>// return this so that we can chain and use the bridge with less code.</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>options: {</code></div><div><code>name: </code><code>"No name"</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>_build: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>//this.$elem.html( "&lt;h1&gt;"+this.options.name+"&lt;/h1&gt;" );</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>myMethod: </code><code>function</code><code>( msg ){</code></div><div><code>// We have direct access to the associated and cached</code></div><div><code>// jQuery element</code></div><div><code>// this.$elem.append( "&lt;p&gt;"+msg+"&lt;/p&gt;" );</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// Object.create support test, and fallback for browsers without it</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( </code><code>typeof</code> <code>Object.create !== </code><code>"function"</code> <code>) {</code></div><div><code>Object.create = </code><code>function</code> <code>(o) {</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>F() {}</code></div><div><code>F.prototype = o;</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>new</code> <code>F();</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Create a plugin based on a defined object</code></div><div><code>$.plugin = </code><code>function</code><code>( name, object ) {</code></div><div><code>$.fn[name] = </code><code>function</code><code>( options ) {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.each(</code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( ! $.data( </code><code>this</code><code>, name ) ) {</code></div><div><code>$.data( </code><code>this</code><code>, name, Object.create( object ).init(</code></div><div><code>options, </code><code>this</code> <code>) );</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Usage:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_188567"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$.plugin( </code><code>"myobj"</code><code>, myObject );</code></div><div><code>$(</code><code>"#elem"</code><code>).myobj( {name: </code><code>"John"</code><code>} );</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>collection = $( </code><code>"#elem"</code> <code>).data( </code><code>"myobj"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>collection.myMethod( </code><code>"I am a method"</code><code>);</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><h4>Further Reading</h4>




<h3>jQuery UI Widget Factory Bridge Pattern</h3>

<p>If you liked the idea of generating plugins based on objects in the last design pattern, then you might be interested in a method found in the jQuery UI Widget Factory called <code>$.widget.bridge</code>.</p>

<p>This bridge basically serves as a middle layer between a JavaScript object that is created using <code>$.widget</code> and the jQuery core API, providing a more built-in solution to achieving object-based plugin definition. Effectively, we’re able to create stateful plugins using a custom constructor.</p>

<p>Moreover,<code>$.widget.bridge</code> provides access to a number of other capabilities, including the following:</p>

<ul>
<li>Both public and private methods are handled as one would expect in classical OOP (i.e. public methods are exposed, while calls to private methods are not possible).</li>

<li>Automatic protection against multiple initializations.</li>

<li>Automatic generation of instances of a passed object, and storage of them within the selection’s internal <code>$.data</code> cache.</li>

<li>Options can be altered post-initialization.</li>
</ul>

<p>For further information on how to use this pattern, please see the inline comments below:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_725852"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>/*!</code></div><div><code>* jQuery UI Widget factory "bridge" plugin boilerplate</code></div><div><code>* Author: @erichynds</code></div><div><code>* Further changes, additional comments: @addyosmani</code></div><div><code>* Licensed under the MIT license</code></div><div><code>*/</code></div><div><code>// a "widgetName" object constructor</code></div><div><code>// required: this must accept two arguments,</code></div><div><code>// options: an object of configuration options</code></div><div><code>// element: the DOM element the instance was created on</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>widgetName = </code><code>function</code><code>( options, element ){</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.name = </code><code>"myWidgetName"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.options = options;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.element = element;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>._init();</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// the "widgetName" prototype</code></div><div><code>widgetName.prototype = {</code></div><div><code>// _create will automatically run the first time this</code></div><div><code>// widget is called</code></div><div><code>_create: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>// creation code</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>// required: initialization logic for the plugin goes into _init</code></div><div><code>// This fires when our instance is first created and when</code></div><div><code>// attempting to initialize the widget again (by the bridge)</code></div><div><code>// after it has already been initialized.</code></div><div><code>_init: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>// init code</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>// required: objects to be used with the bridge must contain an</code></div><div><code>// "option". Post-initialization, the logic for changing options</code></div><div><code>// goes here.</code></div><div><code>option: </code><code>function</code><code>( key, value ){</code></div><div><code>// optional: get/change options post initialization</code></div><div><code>// ignore if you don't require them.</code></div><div><code>// signature: $("#foo").bar({ cool:false });</code></div><div><code>if</code><code>( $.isPlainObject( key ) ){</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.options = $.extend( </code><code>true</code><code>, </code><code>this</code><code>.options, key );</code></div><div><code>// signature: $( "#foo" ).option( "cool" ); - getter</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>else</code> <code>if</code> <code>( key && </code><code>typeof</code> <code>value === </code><code>"undefined"</code> <code>){</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.options[ key ];</code></div><div><code>// signature: $( "#foo" ).bar("option", "baz", false );</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>else</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.options[ key ] = value;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// required: option must return the current instance.</code></div><div><code>// When re-initializing an instance on elements, option</code></div><div><code>// is called first and is then chained to the _init method.</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>// notice no underscore is used for public methods</code></div><div><code>publicFunction: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"public function"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>// underscores are used for private methods</code></div><div><code>_privateFunction: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"private function"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Usage:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_618201"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// connect the widget obj to jQuery's API under the "foo" namespace</code></div><div><code>$.widget.bridge( </code><code>"foo"</code><code>, widgetName );</code></div><div><code>// create an instance of the widget for use</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>instance = $( </code><code>"#foo"</code> <code>).foo({</code></div><div><code>baz: </code><code>true</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>// our widget instance exists in the elem's data</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: #elem</code></div><div><code>console.log(instance.data( </code><code>"foo"</code> <code>).element);</code></div><div><code>// bridge allows us to call public methods</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: "public method"</code></div><div><code>instance.foo(</code><code>"publicFunction"</code><code>);</code></div><div><code>// bridge prevents calls to internal methods</code></div><div><code>instance.foo(</code><code>"_privateFunction"</code><code>);</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><h4>Further Reading</h4>

<ul>
<li>“<a href="http://erichynds.com/jquery/using-jquery-ui-widget-factory-bridge/" target="_blank">Using $.widget.bridge Outside of the Widget Factory</a>,” Eric Hynds</li>
</ul>

<h3>jQuery Mobile Widgets With The Widget factory</h3>

<p>jQuery mobile is a jQuery project framework that encourages the design of ubiquitous web applications that work both on popular mobile devices and platforms and on the desktop. Rather than writing unique applications for each device or OS, we simply write the code once and it should ideally run on many of the A-, B- and C-grade browsers out there at the moment.</p>

<p>The fundamentals behind jQuery mobile can also be applied to plugin and widget development.</p>

<p>What’s interesting in this next pattern is that although there are small, subtle differences in writing a “mobile”-optimized widget, those familiar with using the jQuery UI Widget Factory pattern from earlier should be able to grasp this in next to no time.</p>

<p>The mobile-optimized widget below has a number of interesting differences than the standard UI widget pattern we saw earlier:</p>

<ul>
<li><code>$.mobile.widget</code> is referenced as an existing widget prototype from which to inherit.
