For standard widgets, passing through any such prototype is unnecessary for basic development, but using this jQuery-mobile specific widget prototype provides internal access to further “options” formatting.</li>

<li>In <code>_create()</code>, a guide is provided on how the official jQuery mobile widgets handle element selection, opting for a role-based approach that better fits the jQM mark-up. This isn’t at all to say that standard selection isn’t recommended, only that this approach might make more sense given the structure of jQuery Mobile pages.</li>

<li>Guidelines are also provided in comment form for applying our plugin methods on <code>pagecreate</code> as well as for selecting the plugin application via data roles and data attributes.</li>
</ul>

<div><div id="highlighter_242022"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>/*!</code></div><div><code>* (jQuery mobile) jQuery UI Widget-factory plugin boilerplate (for 1.8/9+)</code></div><div><code>* Author: @scottjehl</code></div><div><code>* Further changes: @addyosmani</code></div><div><code>* Licensed under the MIT license</code></div><div><code>*/</code></div><div><code>;(</code><code>function</code> <code>( $, window, document, undefined ) {</code></div><div><code>// define a widget under a namespace of our choice</code></div><div><code>// here "mobile" has been used in the first argument</code></div><div><code>$.widget( </code><code>"mobile.widgetName"</code><code>, $.mobile.widget, {</code></div><div><code>// Options to be used as defaults</code></div><div><code>options: {</code></div><div><code>foo: </code><code>true</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>bar: </code><code>false</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>_create: </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>// _create will automatically run the first time this</code></div><div><code>// widget is called. Put the initial widget set-up code</code></div><div><code>// here, then we can access the element on which</code></div><div><code>// the widget was called via this.element</code></div><div><code>// The options defined above can be accessed via</code></div><div><code>// this.options</code></div><div><code>// var m = this.element,</code></div><div><code>// p = m.parents( ":jqmData(role="page")" ),</code></div><div><code>// c = p.find( ":jqmData(role="content")" )</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>// Private methods/props start with underscores</code></div><div><code>_dosomething: </code><code>function</code><code>(){ ... },</code></div><div><code>// Public methods like these below can can be called</code></div><div><code>// externally:</code></div><div><code>// $("#myelem").foo( "enable", arguments );</code></div><div><code>enable: </code><code>function</code><code>() { ... },</code></div><div><code>// Destroy an instantiated plugin and clean up modifications</code></div><div><code>// the widget has made to the DOM</code></div><div><code>destroy: </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>// this.element.removeStuff();</code></div><div><code>// For UI 1.8, destroy must be invoked from the</code></div><div><code>// base widget</code></div><div><code>$.Widget.prototype.destroy.call( </code><code>this</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// For UI 1.9, define _destroy instead and don't</code></div><div><code>// worry about calling the base widget</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>methodB: </code><code>function</code> <code>( event ) {</code></div><div><code>//_trigger dispatches callbacks the plugin user can</code></div><div><code>// subscribe to</code></div><div><code>// signature: _trigger( "callbackName", [eventObject],</code></div><div><code>// [uiObject] )</code></div><div><code>// e.g. this._trigger( "hover", e /*where e.type ==</code></div><div><code>// "mouseenter"*/, { hovered: $(e.target)});</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>._trigger( </code><code>"methodA"</code><code>, event, {</code></div><div><code>key: value</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>methodA: </code><code>function</code> <code>( event ) {</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>._trigger( </code><code>"dataChanged"</code><code>, event, {</code></div><div><code>key: value</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>// Respond to any changes the user makes to the option method</code></div><div><code>_setOption: </code><code>function</code> <code>( key, value ) {</code></div><div><code>switch</code> <code>( key ) {</code></div><div><code>case</code> <code>"someValue"</code><code>:</code></div><div><code>// this.options.someValue = doSomethingWith( value );</code></div><div><code>break</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>default</code><code>:</code></div><div><code>// this.options[ key ] = value;</code></div><div><code>break</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// For UI 1.8, _setOption must be manually invoked from</code></div><div><code>// the base widget</code></div><div><code>$.Widget.prototype._setOption.apply(</code><code>this</code><code>, arguments);</code></div><div><code>// For UI 1.9 the _super method can be used instead</code></div><div><code>// this._super( "_setOption", key, value );</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>})( jQuery, window, document );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Usage:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_534092"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>instance = $( </code><code>"#foo"</code> <code>).widgetName({</code></div><div><code>foo: </code><code>false</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>instance.widgetName( </code><code>"methodB"</code> <code>);</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><p>We can also self-initialize this widget whenever a new page in jQuery Mobile is created. jQuery Mobile's <em>page</em> plugin dispatches a <em>create</em> event when a jQuery Mobile page (found via the <code>data-role="page"</code> attribute) is first initialized. We can listen for that event (called "pagecreate") and run our plugin automatically whenever a new page is created.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_211872"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$(document).on(</code><code>"pagecreate"</code><code>, </code><code>function</code> <code>( e ) {</code></div><div><code>// In here, e.target refers to the page that was created</code></div><div><code>// (it's the target of the pagecreate event)</code></div><div><code>// So, we can simply find elements on this page that match a</code></div><div><code>// selector of our choosing, and call our plugin on them.</code></div><div><code>// Here's how we'd call our "foo" plugin on any element with a</code></div><div><code>// data-role attribute of "foo":</code></div><div><code>$(e.target).find( </code><code>"[data-role="</code><code>foo</code><code>"]"</code> <code>).foo( options );</code></div><div><code>// Or, better yet, let's write the selector accounting for the configurable</code></div><div><code>// data-attribute namespace</code></div><div><code>$( e.target ).find( </code><code>":jqmData(role="</code><code>foo</code><code>")"</code> <code>).foo( options );</code></div><div><code>});</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><p>We can now simply reference the script containing our widget and <code>pagecreate</code> binding in a page running jQuery Mobile site, and it will automatically run like any other jQuery Mobile plugin.</p>
<h3>RequireJS And The jQuery UI Widget Factory</h3>

<p>As we covered in the section on <em>Modern Module Design Patterns</em>, RequireJS is an AMD-compatible script loader that provides a clean solution for encapsulating application logic inside manageable modules.</p>

<p>It’s able to load modules in the correct order (through its order plugin), simplifies the process of combining scripts via its excellent r.js optimizer and provides the means for defining dynamic dependencies on a per-module basis.</p>

<p>In the boilerplate pattern below, we demonstrate how an AMD (and thus RequireJS) compatible jQuery UI widget can be defined that does the following:</p>

<ul>
<li>Allows the definition of widget module dependencies, building on top of the previous jQuery UI Widget Factory pattern presented earlier.</li>

<li>Demonstrates one approach to passing in HTML template assets for creating templated widgets (using Underscore.js micro-templating).</li>

<li>Includes a quick tip on adjustments that we can make to our widget module if we wish to later pass it through to the RequireJS optimizer.</li>
</ul>

<div><div id="highlighter_470202"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>/*!</code></div><div><code>* jQuery UI Widget + RequireJS module boilerplate (for 1.8/9+)</code></div><div><code>* Authors: @jrburke, @addyosmani</code></div><div><code>* Licensed under the MIT license</code></div><div><code>*/</code></div><div><code>// Note from James:</code></div><div><code>//</code></div><div><code>// This assumes we are using the RequireJS+jQuery file, and</code></div><div><code>// that the following files are all in the same directory:</code></div><div><code>//</code></div><div><code>// - require-jquery.js</code></div><div><code>// - jquery-ui.custom.min.js (custom jQuery UI build with widget factory)</code></div><div><code>// - templates/</code></div><div><code>// - asset.html</code></div><div><code>// - ao.myWidget.js</code></div><div><code>// Then we can construct the widget as follows:</code></div><div><code>// ao.myWidget.js file:</code></div><div><code>define( </code><code>"ao.myWidget"</code><code>, [</code><code>"jquery"</code><code>, </code><code>"text!templates/asset.html"</code><code>, </code><code>"underscore"</code><code>, </code><code>"jquery-ui.custom.min"</code><code>], </code><code>function</code> <code>( $, assetHtml, _ ) {</code></div><div><code>// define our widget under a namespace of our choice</code></div><div><code>// "ao" is used here as a demonstration</code></div><div><code>$.widget( </code><code>"ao.myWidget"</code><code>, {</code></div><div><code>// Options to be used as defaults</code></div><div><code>options: {},</code></div><div><code>// Set up widget (e.g. create element, apply theming,</code></div><div><code>// bind events, etc.)</code></div><div><code>_create: </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>// _create will automatically run the first time</code></div><div><code>// this widget is called. Put the initial widget</code></div><div><code>// set-up code here, then we can access the element</code></div><div><code>// on which the widget was called via this.element.</code></div><div><code>// The options defined above can be accessed via</code></div><div><code>// this.options</code></div><div><code>// this.element.addStuff();</code></div><div><code>// this.element.addStuff();</code></div><div><code>// We can then use Underscore templating with</code></div><div><code>// with the assetHtml that has been pulled in</code></div><div><code>// var template = _.template( assetHtml );</code></div><div><code>// this.content.append( template({}) );</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>// Destroy an instantiated plugin and clean up modifications</code></div><div><code>// that the widget has made to the DOM</code></div><div><code>destroy: </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>// this.element.removeStuff();</code></div><div><code>// For UI 1.8, destroy must be invoked from the base</code></div><div><code>// widget</code></div><div><code>$.Widget.prototype.destroy.call( </code><code>this</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// For UI 1.9, define _destroy instead and don't worry</code></div><div><code>// about calling the base widget</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>methodB: </code><code>function</code> <code>( event ) {</code></div><div><code>// _trigger dispatches callbacks the plugin user can</code></div><div><code>// subscribe to</code></div><div><code>// signature: _trigger( "callbackName", [eventObject],</code></div><div><code>// [uiObject] )</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>._trigger( </code><code>"methodA"</code><code>, event, {</code></div><div><code>key: value</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>methodA: </code><code>function</code> <code>( event ) {</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>._trigger(</code><code>"dataChanged"</code><code>, event, {</code></div><div><code>key: value</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>// Respond to any changes the user makes to the option method</code></div><div><code>_setOption: </code><code>function</code> <code>( key, value ) {</code></div><div><code>switch</code> <code>(key) {</code></div><div><code>case</code> <code>"someValue"</code><code>:</code></div><div><code>// this.options.someValue = doSomethingWith( value );</code></div><div><code>break</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>default</code><code>:</code></div><div><code>// this.options[ key ] = value;</code></div><div><code>break</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// For UI 1.8, _setOption must be manually invoked from</code></div><div><code>// the base widget</code></div><div><code>$.Widget.prototype._setOption.apply( </code><code>this</code><code>, arguments );</code></div><div><code>// For UI 1.9 the _super method can be used instead</code></div><div><code>// this._super( "_setOption", key, value );</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>});</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Usage:</p>

<p>index.html:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_500086"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>&lt;script data-main=</code><code>"scripts/main"</code> <code>src=</code><code>"<a href="http://requirejs.org/docs/release/1.0.1/minified/require.js" target="_blank">http://requirejs.org/docs/release/1.0.1/minified/require.js</a>"</code><code>&gt;&lt;/script&gt;</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><p>main.js</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_325646"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>require({</code></div><div><code>paths: {</code></div><div><code>"jquery"</code><code>: </code><code>"<a href="https://ajax.googleapis.com/ajax/libs/jquery/1.7.1/jquery.min" target="_blank">https://ajax.googleapis.com/ajax/libs/jquery/1.7.1/jquery.min</a>"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>"jqueryui"</code><code>: </code><code>"<a href="https://ajax.googleapis.com/ajax/libs/jqueryui/1.8.18/jquery-ui.min" target="_blank">https://ajax.googleapis.com/ajax/libs/jqueryui/1.8.18/jquery-ui.min</a>"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>"boilerplate"</code><code>: </code><code>"../patterns/jquery.widget-factory.requirejs.boilerplate"</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}, [</code><code>"require"</code><code>, </code><code>"jquery"</code><code>, </code><code>"jqueryui"</code><code>, </code><code>"boilerplate"</code><code>],</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>(req, $) {</code></div><div><code>$(</code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>instance = $(</code><code>"#elem"</code><code>).myWidget();</code></div><div><code>instance.myWidget(</code><code>"methodB"</code><code>);</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>});</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><h4>Further Reading</h4>



<h3>Globally And Per-Call Overridable Options (Best Options Pattern)</h3>

<p>For our next pattern, we’ll look at an optimal approach to configuring options and defaults for a plugin. The way most of us are probably familiar with defining plugin options is to pass through an object literal of defaults to <code>$.extend()</code>, as demonstrated in our basic plugin boilerplate.</p>

<p>If, however, we’re working with a plugin with many customizable options that we would like users to be able to override either globally or on a per-call level, then we can structure things a little more optimally.</p>

<p>Instead, by referring to an options object defined within the plugin namespace explicitly (for example, <code>$fn.pluginName.options</code>) and merging this with any options passed through to the plugin when it is initially invoked, users have the option of either passing options through during plugin initialization or overriding options outside of the plugin (as demonstrated here).</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_952967"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>/*!</code></div><div><code>* jQuery "best options" plugin boilerplate</code></div><div><code>* Author: @cowboy</code></div><div><code>* Further changes: @addyosmani</code></div><div><code>* Licensed under the MIT license</code></div><div><code>*/</code></div><div><code>;(</code><code>function</code> <code>( $, window, document, undefined ) {</code></div><div><code>$.fn.pluginName = </code><code>function</code> <code>( options ) {</code></div><div><code>// Here's a best practice for overriding "defaults"</code></div><div><code>// with specified options. Note how, rather than a</code></div><div><code>// regular defaults object being passed as the second</code></div><div><code>// parameter, we instead refer to $.fn.pluginName.options</code></div><div><code>// explicitly, merging it with the options passed directly</code></div><div><code>// to the plugin. This allows us to override options both</code></div><div><code>// globally and on a per-call level.</code></div><div><code>options = $.extend( {}, $.fn.pluginName.options, options );</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.each(</code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>elem = $(</code><code>this</code><code>);</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// Globally overriding options</code></div><div><code>// Here are our publicly accessible default plugin options</code></div><div><code>// that are available in case the user doesn't pass in all</code></div><div><code>// of the values expected. The user is given a default</code></div><div><code>// experience but can also override the values as necessary.</code></div><div><code>// e.g. $fn.pluginName.key ="otherval";</code></div><div><code>$.fn.pluginName.options = {</code></div><div><code>key: </code><code>"value"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>myMethod: </code><code>function</code> <code>( elem, param ) {</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>})( jQuery, window, document );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Usage:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_322369"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$(</code><code>"#elem"</code><code>).pluginName({</code></div><div><code>key: </code><code>"foobar"</code></div><div><code>});</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><h4>Further Reading</h4>



<h3>A Highly Configurable And Mutable Plugin Pattern</h3>

<p>In this pattern, similar to Alex Sexton's prototypal inheritance plugin pattern, logic for our plugin isn’t nested in a jQuery plugin itself. We instead define our plugin logic using a constructor and an object literal defined on its prototype. jQuery is then used for the actual instantiation of the plugin object.</p>

<p>Customization is taken to the next level by employing two little tricks, one of which we’ve seen in previous patterns:</p>

<ul>
<li>Options can be overridden both globally and per collection of elements/</li>

<li>Options can be customized on a <strong>per-element</strong> level through HTML5 data attributes (as shown below). This facilitates plugin behavior that can be applied to a collection of elements but then customized inline without the need to instantiate each element with a different default value. </li>
</ul>

<p>We don’t see the latter option in the wild too often, but it can be a significantly cleaner solution (as long as we don’t mind the inline approach). If wondering where this could be useful, imagine writing a draggable plugin for a large set of elements. We could go about customizing their options as follows:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_619395"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$( </code><code>".item-a"</code> <code>).draggable( {</code><code>"defaultPosition"</code><code>:</code><code>"top-left"</code><code>} );</code></div><div><code>$( </code><code>".item-b"</code> <code>).draggable( {</code><code>"defaultPosition"</code><code>:</code><code>"bottom-right"</code><code>} );</code></div><div><code>$( </code><code>".item-c"</code> <code>).draggable( {</code><code>"defaultPosition"</code><code>:</code><code>"bottom-left"</code><code>} );</code></div><div><code>//etc</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>But using our patterns inline approach, the following would be possible:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_43155"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$( </code><code>".items"</code> <code>).draggable();</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<div><div id="highlighter_309546"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>html</code></div><div><code>&lt;li </code><code>class</code><code>=</code><code>"item"</code> <code>data-plugin-options=</code><code>"{"</code><code>defaultPosition</code><code>":"</code><code>top-left</code><code>"}"</code><code>&gt;&lt;/div&gt;</code></div><div><code>&lt;li </code><code>class</code><code>=</code><code>"item"</code> <code>data-plugin-options=</code><code>"{"</code><code>defaultPosition</code><code>":"</code><code>bottom-left</code><code>"}"</code><code>&gt;&lt;/div&gt;</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>And so on. We may well have a preference for one of these approaches, but it is just another variation worth being aware of.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_6205"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>/*</code></div><div><code>* "Highly configurable" mutable plugin boilerplate</code></div><div><code>* Author: @markdalgleish</code></div><div><code>* Further changes, comments: @addyosmani</code></div><div><code>* Licensed under the MIT license</code></div><div><code>*/</code></div><div><code>// Note that with this pattern, as per Alex Sexton's, the plugin logic</code></div><div><code>// hasn't been nested in a jQuery plugin. Instead, we just use</code></div><div><code>// jQuery for its instantiation.</code></div><div><code>;(</code><code>function</code><code>( $, window, document, undefined ){</code></div><div><code>// our plugin constructor</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>Plugin = </code><code>function</code><code>( elem, options ){</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.elem = elem;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.$elem = $(elem);</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.options = options;</code></div><div><code>// This next line takes advantage of HTML5 data attributes</code></div><div><code>// to support customization of the plugin on a per-element</code></div><div><code>// basis. For example,</code></div><div><code>// &lt;div class="item" data-plugin-options="{'message':'Goodbye World!'}"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.metadata = </code><code>this</code><code>.$elem.data( </code><code>"plugin-options"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// the plugin prototype</code></div><div><code>Plugin.prototype = {</code></div><div><code>defaults: {</code></div><div><code>message: </code><code>"Hello world!"</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>init: </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>// Introduce defaults that can be extended either</code></div><div><code>// globally or using an object literal.</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.config = $.extend( {}, </code><code>this</code><code>.defaults, </code><code>this</code><code>.options,</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.metadata );</code></div><div><code>// Sample usage:</code></div><div><code>// Set the message per instance:</code></div><div><code>// $( "#elem" ).plugin( { message: "Goodbye World!"} );</code></div><div><code>// or</code></div><div><code>// var p = new Plugin( document.getElementById( "elem" ),</code></div><div><code>// { message: "Goodbye World!"}).init()</code></div><div><code>// or, set the global default message:</code></div><div><code>// Plugin.defaults.message = "Goodbye World!"</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.sampleMethod();</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>sampleMethod: </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>// e.g. show the currently configured message</code></div><div><code>// console.log(this.config.message);</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>Plugin.defaults = Plugin.prototype.defaults;</code></div><div><code>$.fn.plugin = </code><code>function</code><code>( options ) {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>this</code><code>.each(</code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>new</code> <code>Plugin( </code><code>this</code><code>, options ).init();</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// optional: window.Plugin = Plugin;</code></div><div><code>})( jQuery, window, document );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Usage:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_527696"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>$(</code><code>"#elem"</code><code>).plugin({</code></div><div><code>message: </code><code>"foobar"</code></div><div><code>});</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><h4>Further Reading</h4>



<h3>What Makes A Good Plugin Beyond Patterns?</h3>

<p>At the end of the day, design patterns are just one facet to writing maintainable jQuery plugins. There are a number of other factors worth considering and I would like to share my own criteria for selecting third-party plugins to address some of the other concerns. I hope this helps increase the overall quality of your plugin projects:</p>

<p><strong>Quality</strong></p>
<p>Adhere to best practices with respect to both the JavaScript and jQuery that you write. Are efforts being made to lint the plugin code using either jsHint or jsLint? Is the plugin written optimally?</p>

<p><strong>Code Style</strong></p>
<p>Does the plugin follow a consistent code style guide such as the <a href="http://docs.jquery.com/JQuery_Core_Style_Guidelines" target="_blank">jQuery Core Style Guidelines</a>? If not, is your code at least relatively clean and readable?</p>

<p><strong>Compatibility</strong></p><p>Which versions of jQuery is the plugin compatible with? Has it been tested with the latest jQuery-git builds or latest stable? If the plugin was written before jQuery 1.6, then it might have issues with attributes and properties, because the way they were approached changed in that release.</p>

<p>New versions of jQuery offer improvements and opportunities for the jQuery project to improve on what the core library offers. With this comes occasional breakages (mainly in major releases) as we move towards a better way of doing things. I’d like to see plugin authors update their code when necessary or, at a minimum, test their plugins with new versions to make sure everything works as expected.</p>

<p><strong>Reliability</strong></p><p>The plugin should come with its own set of unit tests. Not only do these prove it actually functions as expected, but they can also improve the design without breaking it for end users. I consider unit tests essential for any serious jQuery plugin that is meant for a production environment, and they’re not that hard to write. For an excellent guide to automated JavaScript testing with QUnit, you may be interested in “<a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/scriptjunkie/gg749824" target="_blank">Automating JavaScript Testing With QUnit</a>,” by <a href="http://bassistance.de/" target="_blank">Jörn Zaefferer</a>.</p>

<p><strong>Performance</strong></p><p>If the plugin needs to perform tasks that require extensive processing or heavily manipulation of the DOM, one should follow best practices for benchmarking to help minimize this. Use <a href="http://jsperf.com" target="_blank">jsPerf.com</a> to test segments of the code to a) how well it performs in different browsers and b) discover what, if anything, might be optimized further.</p>

<p><strong>Documentation</strong></p><p>If the intension is for other developers to use the plugin, ensure that it’s well documented. Document the API and how the plugin is to be used. What methods and options does the plugin support? Does it have any gotchas that users need to be aware of? If users cannot figure out how to use the plugin, they’ll likely look for an alternative. It is also of great help to comment your plugin code. This is by far the best gift you can offer other developers. If someone feels they can navigate your code base well enough to use it or improve it, then you’ve done a good job.</p>

<p><strong>Likelihood of maintenance</strong></p><p>When releasing a plugin, estimate how much time may be required for maintenance and support. We all love to share our plugins with the community, but one needs to set expectations for ones ability to answer questions, address issues and make continuous improvements. This can be done simply by stating the project intentions for maintenance support upfront in the <em>README</em> file.</p>

<h2>Conclusions</h2>

<p>In this chapter, we explored several time-saving design patterns and best practices that can be employed to improve how jQuery plugins can be written. Some are better suited to certain use cases than others, but I hope on the whole these patterns are useful.</p>

<p>Remember, when selecting a pattern, it is important to be practical. Don’t use a plugin pattern just for the sake of it, rather, invest time in understanding the underlying structure, and establish how well it solves your problem or fits the component you’re trying to build. </p>


<h2 id="detailnamespacing">Namespacing Patterns</h2>

<p>In this section, we're going to explore patterns for namespacing in JavaScript. Namespaces can be considered a logical grouping of units of code under a unique identifier. The identifier can be referenced in many namespaces and each identifier can itself contain a hierarchy of its own nested (or sub) namespaces.</p>

<p>In application development, we employ namespaces for a number of important reasons. In JavaScript, they help us avoid <b>collisions</b> with other objects or variables in the global namespace. They're also extremely useful for helping organize blocks of functionality in a code-base so that it can be more easily referenced and used.</p>

<p>Namespacing any serious script or application is critical as it's important to safeguard our code from breaking in the event of another script on the page using the <b>same</b> variable or method names we are. With the number of <b>third-party</b> tags regularly injected into pages these days, this can be a common problem we all need to tackle at some point in our careers. As a well-behaved "citizen" of the global namespace, it's also imperative that we do our best to similarly not prevent other developer's scripts executing due to the same issues.</p>

<p>Whilst JavaScript doesn't really have built-in support for namespaces like other languages, it does have objects and closures which can be used to achieve a similar effect.</p>

<h2>Namespacing Fundamentals</h2>
<p>Namespaces can be found in almost any serious JavaScript application. Unless we're working with a simple code-snippet, it's imperative that we do our best to ensure that we're implementing namespacing correctly as it's not just simple to pick-up, it'll also avoid third party code clobbering our own. The patterns we'll be examining in this section are:</p>
<ol>
  <li>Single global variables</li>
  <li>Prefix namespacing</li>
  <li>Object literal notation</li>
  <li>Nested namespacing</li>
  <li>Immediately-invoked Function Expressions</li>
  <li>Namespace injection</li>
</ol>
<h3>1. Single global variables</h3>
<p>One popular pattern for namespacing in JavaScript is opting for a single global variable as our primary object of reference. A skeleton implementation of this where we return an object with functions and properties can be found below:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_472254"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>myApplication = (</code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>//...</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>//...</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>})();</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>Although this works for certain situations, the biggest challenge with the single global variable pattern is ensuring that no one else has used the same global variable name as we have in the page.</p>

<h3>2. Prefix namespacing </h3>

<p>One solution to the above problem, as mentioned by <a href="http://michaux.ca/articles/javascript-namespacing" target="_blank">Peter Michaux</a>, is to use prefix namespacing. It's a simple concept at heart, but the idea is we select a unique prefix namespace we wish to use (in this example, <code>myApplication_</code>) and then define any methods, variables or other objects after the prefix as follows:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_618144"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>myApplication_propertyA = {};</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>myApplication_propertyB = {};</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>myApplication_myMethod() {</code></div><div><code>//...</code></div><div><code>}</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>This is effective from the perspective of decreasing the chances of a particular variable existing in the global scope, but remember that a uniquely named object can have the same effect.</p>

<p>This aside, the biggest issue with the pattern is that it can result in a large number of global objects once our application starts to grow. There is also quite a heavy reliance on our prefix not being used by any other developers in the global namespace, so be careful if opting to use this.</p>

<p>For more on Peter's views about the single global variable pattern, read his excellent post on them <a href="http://peter.michaux.ca/articles/javascript-namespacing" target="_blank">http://peter.michaux.ca/articles/javascript-namespacing</a>.</p>

<h3>3. Object literal notation</h3>
<p>Object literal notation (which we also cover in the module pattern section of the book) can be thought of as an object containing a collection of key:value pairs with a colon separating each pair of keys and values where keys can also represent new namespaces.</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_131865"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>myApplication = {</code></div><div><code>// As we've seen, we can easily define functionality for</code></div><div><code>// this object literal..</code></div><div><code>getInfo: </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>//...</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>// but we can also populate it to support</code></div><div><code>// further object namespaces containing anything</code></div><div><code>// anything we wish:</code></div><div><code>models: {},</code></div><div><code>views: {</code></div><div><code>pages: {}</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>collections: {}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>One can also opt for adding properties directly to the namespace:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_669238"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>myApplication.foo = </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>"bar"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>myApplication.utils = {</code></div><div><code>toString: </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>//...</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>export</code><code>: </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>//...</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>Object literals have the advantage of not polluting the global namespace but assist in organizing code and parameters logically. They are truly beneficial if we wish to create easily-readable structures that can be expanded to support deep nesting. Unlike simple global variables, object literals often also take into account tests for the existence of a variable by the same name so the chances of collision occurring are significantly reduced.</p>

<p>In the next sample, we demonstrate a number of different ways in which we can check to see if a variable (object or plugin namespace) already exists, defining it if it doesn't.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_710928"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// This doesn't check for existence of "myApplication" in</code></div><div><code>// the global namespace. Bad practice as we can easily</code></div><div><code>// clobber an existing variable/namespace with the same name</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>myApplication = {};</code></div><div><code>// The following options *do* check for variable/namespace existence.</code></div><div><code>// If already defined, we use that instance, otherwise we assign a new</code></div><div><code>// object literal to myApplication.</code></div><div><code>//</code></div><div><code>// Option 1: var myApplication = myApplication || {};</code></div><div><code>// Option 2: if( !MyApplication ){ MyApplication = {} };</code></div><div><code>// Option 3: window.myApplication || ( window.myApplication = {} );</code></div><div><code>// Option 4: var myApplication = $.fn.myApplication = function() {};</code></div><div><code>// Option 5: var myApplication = myApplication === undefined ? {} : myApplication;</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>We'll often see developers opting for Option 1 or Option 2 - they are both straight-forward to understand and are equivalent in terms of their end-result.<p>Option 3 assumes that we're working in the global namespace, but it could also be written as:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_298765"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>myApplication || (myApplication = {});</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>This variation assumes that <code>myApplication</code> has already been initialized and so it's only really useful for a parameter/argument scenario as in the following example:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_847866"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>function</code> <code>foo() {</code></div><div><code>myApplication || ( myApplication = {} );</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// myApplication hasn't been initialized,</code></div><div><code>// so foo() throws a ReferenceError</code></div><div><code>foo();</code></div><div><code>// However accepting myApplication as an</code></div><div><code>// argument</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>foo( myApplication ) {</code></div><div><code>myApplication || ( myApplication = {} );</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>foo();</code></div><div><code>// Even if myApplication === undefined, there is no error</code></div><div><code>// and myApplication gets set to {} correctly</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Options 4 can be useful for writing jQuery plugins where:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_832044"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// If we were to define a new plugin..</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>myPlugin = $.fn.myPlugin = </code><code>function</code><code>() { ... };</code></div><div><code>// Then later rather than having to type:</code></div><div><code>$.fn.myPlugin.defaults = {};</code></div><div><code>// We can do:</code></div><div><code>myPlugin.defaults = {};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>This results in better compression (minification) and can save on scope lookups.</p>

<p>Option 5 is a little similar to Option 4, but is a long-form which evaluates whether <code>myApplication</code> is <code>undefined</code> inline such that it's defined as an object if not, otherwise set to an existing value for <code>myApplication</code> if so.</p>It is shown just for the sake of being thorough but in most situations, Options 1-4 will more than suffice for most needs.<p>There is of course a great deal of variance in how and where object literals are used for organizing and structuring code. For smaller applications wishing to expose a nested API for a particular self-enclosed module, we may just find ourselves using the Revealing Module Pattern, which we covered earlier in the book:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_18997"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>namespace = (</code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>// defined within the local scope</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>privateMethod1 = </code><code>function</code> <code>() { </code><code>/* ... */</code> <code>},</code></div><div><code>privateMethod2 = </code><code>function</code> <code>() { </code><code>/* ... */</code> <code>},</code></div><div><code>privateProperty1 = </code><code>"foobar"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>// the object literal returned here can have as many</code></div><div><code>// nested depths as we wish, however as mentioned,</code></div><div><code>// this way of doing things works best for smaller,</code></div><div><code>// limited-scope applications in my personal opinion</code></div><div><code>publicMethod1: privateMethod1,</code></div><div><code>// nested namespace with public properties</code></div><div><code>properties: {</code></div><div><code>publicProperty1: privateProperty1</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>// another tested namespace</code></div><div><code>utils: {</code></div><div><code>publicMethod2: privateMethod2</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>...</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>})();</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>The benefit of object literals is that they offer us a very elegant key/value syntax to work with; one where we're able to easily encapsulate any distinct logic or functionality for our application in a way that clearly separates it from others and provides a solid foundation for extending our code.</p>
<p>A possible downside however is that object literals have the potential to grow into long syntactic constructs. Opting to take advantage of the nested namespace pattern (which also uses the same pattern as its base)</p>
<p>This pattern has a number of other useful applications too. In addition to namespacing, it's often of benefit to decouple the default configuration for our application into a single area that can be easily modified without the need to search through our entire codebase just to alter them - object literals work great for this purpose. Here's an example of a hypothetical object literal for configuration:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_945024"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>myConfig = {</code></div><div><code>language: </code><code>"english"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>defaults: {</code></div><div><code>enableGeolocation: </code><code>true</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>enableSharing: </code><code>false</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>maxPhotos: 20</code></div><div><code>},</code></div><div><code>theme: {</code></div><div><code>skin: </code><code>"a"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>toolbars: {</code></div><div><code>index: </code><code>"ui-navigation-toolbar"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>pages: </code><code>"ui-custom-toolbar"</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>Note that JSON is a subset of object literal notation and there are really only minor syntactical differences between it and the above (e.g JSON keys must be strings). If for any reason one wishes to use JSON for storing configuration data instead (e.g. for simpler storage when sending to the back-end), feel free to. For more on the object literal pattern, I recommend reading Rebecca Murphey's excellent <a href="http://rmurphey.com/blog/2009/10/15/using-objects-to-organize-your-code/" target="_blank">article</a> on the topic as she covers a few areas we didn't touch upon.</p>
<h3>4. Nested namespacing</h3>
<p>An extension of the object literal pattern is nested namespacing. It's another common pattern used that offers a lower risk of collision due to the fact that even if a namespace already exists, it's unlikely the same nested children do.</p>
<p>Does this look familiar?</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_510110"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>YAHOO.util.Dom.getElementsByClassName(</code><code>"test"</code><code>);</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>Older versions of Yahoo!'s YUI library use the nested object namespacing pattern regularly. During my time as an engineer at AOL, we also used this pattern in many of our larger applications. A sample implementation of nested namespacing may look like this:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_439010"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>myApp = myApp || {};</code></div><div><code>// perform a similar existence check when defining nested</code></div><div><code>// children</code></div><div><code>myApp.routers = myApp.routers || {};</code></div><div><code>myApp.model = myApp.model || {};</code></div><div><code>myApp.model.special = myApp.model.special || {};</code></div><div><code>// nested namespaces can be as complex as required:</code></div><div><code>// myApp.utilities.charting.html5.plotGraph(/*..*/);</code></div><div><code>// myApp.modules.financePlanner.getSummary();</code></div><div><code>// myApp.services.social.facebook.realtimeStream.getLatest();</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p><strong>Note: The above differs from how YUI3 approaches namespacing as modules there use a sandboxed API host object with far less and far shallower namespacing.</strong></p>

<p>We can also opt to declare new nested namespaces/properties as indexed properties as follows:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_561202"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>myApp[</code><code>"routers"</code><code>] = myApp[</code><code>"routers"</code><code>] || {};</code></div><div><code>myApp[</code><code>"models"</code><code>] = myApp[</code><code>"models"</code><code>] || {};</code></div><div><code>myApp[</code><code>"controllers"</code><code>] = myApp[</code><code>"controllers"</code><code>] || {};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>Both options are readable, organized and offer a relatively safe way of namespacing our application in a similar fashion to what we may be used to in other languages. The only real caveat however is that it requires our browser's JavaScript engine first locating the myApp object and then digging down until it gets to the function we actually wish to use.</p>
<p>This can mean an increased amount of work to perform lookups, however developers such as <a href="http://twitter.com/kangax" target="_blank">Juriy Zaytsev</a> have previously tested and found the performance differences between single object namespacing vs the "nested" approach to be quite negligible.</p>

<h3>5. Immediately-invoked Function Expressions (IIFE)s</h3>

<p>Earlier in the book, we briefly covered the concept of an <a href="http://benalman.com/news/2010/11/immediately-invoked-function-expression/" target="_blank">IIFE</a> (immediately-invoked function expression) which is effectively an unnamed function, immediately invoked after it's been defined. If it sounds familiar it's because you may have previous come across it referred to as a self-executing (or self-invoked) anonymous function, however I personally feel Ben Alman's IIFE naming is more accurate. In JavaScript, because both variables and functions explicitly defined within such a context may only be accessed inside of it, function invocation provides an easy means to achieving privacy.</p>

<p>IIFEs are a popular approach to encapsulating application logic to protect it from the global namespace but also have their use in the world of namespacing.</p>

<p>Examples of IIFEs can be found below:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_550056"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// an (anonymous) immediately-invoked function expression</code></div><div><code>(</code><code>function</code> <code>() { </code><code>/*...*/</code> <code>})();</code></div><div><code>// a named immediately-invoked function expression</code></div><div><code>(</code><code>function</code> <code>foobar () { </code><code>/*..*/</code> <code>})();</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Examples of self-executing functions, which are quite different than IIFEs, can be found below:</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_24871"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// named self-executing function</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>foobar () { foobar(); }</code></div><div><code>// anonymous self-executing function</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>foobar = </code><code>function</code> <code>() { arguments.callee(); }</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>Back to the IIFEs, a slightly more expanded version of the first IIFE example might look like:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_828425"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>namespace = namespace || {};</code></div><div><code>// here a namespace object is passed as a function</code></div><div><code>// parameter, where we assign public methods and</code></div><div><code>// properties to it</code></div><div><code>(</code><code>function</code><code>( o ) {</code></div><div><code>o.foo = </code><code>"foo"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>o.bar = </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>"bar"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>})( namespace );</code></div><div><code>console.log( namespace );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>Whilst readable, this example could be significantly expanded on to address common development concerns such as defined levels of privacy (public/private functions and variables) as well as convenient namespace extension. Let's go through some more code:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_765717"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// namespace (our namespace name) and undefined are passed here</code></div><div><code>// to ensure 1. namespace can be modified locally and isn't</code></div><div><code>// overwritten outside of our function context</code></div><div><code>// 2. the value of undefined is guaranteed as being truly</code></div><div><code>// undefined. This is to avoid issues with undefined being</code></div><div><code>// mutable pre-ES5.</code></div><div><code>;(</code><code>function</code> <code>( namespace, undefined ) {</code></div><div><code>// private properties</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>foo = </code><code>"foo"</code><code>,</code></div><div><code>bar = </code><code>"bar"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>// public methods and properties</code></div><div><code>namespace.foobar = </code><code>"foobar"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>namespace.say = </code><code>function</code> <code>( msg ) {</code></div><div><code>speak( msg );</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>namespace.sayHello = </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>namespace.say( </code><code>"hello world"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// private method</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>speak(msg) {</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"You said: "</code> <code>+ msg );</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// check to evaluate whether "namespace" exists in the</code></div><div><code>// global namespace - if not, assign window.namespace an</code></div><div><code>// object literal</code></div><div><code>})( window.namespace = window.namespace || {} );</code></div><div><code>// we can then test our properties and methods as follows</code></div><div><code>// public</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: foobar</code></div><div><code>console.log( namespace.foobar );</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: You said: hello world</code></div><div><code>namespace.sayHello();</code></div><div><code>// assigning new properties</code></div><div><code>namespace.foobar2 = </code><code>"foobar"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: foobar</code></div><div><code>console.log( namespace.foobar2 );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>Extensibility is of course key to any scalable namespacing pattern and IIFEs can be used to achieve this quite easily. In the below example, our "namespace" is once again passed as an argument to our anonymous function and is then extended (or decorated) with further functionality:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_72135"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// let's extend the namespace with new functionality</code></div><div><code>(</code><code>function</code><code>( namespace, undefined ) {</code></div><div><code>// public method</code></div><div><code>namespace.sayGoodbye = </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>namespace.say( </code><code>"goodbye"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>})( window.namespace = window.namespace || {});</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: goodbye</code></div><div><code>namespace.sayGoodbye();</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>If you would like to find out more about this pattern, I recommend reading Ben's <a href="http://benalman.com/news/2010/11/immediately-invoked-function-expression/" target="_blank">IIFE post</a> for more information.</p>

<h3>6. Namespace injection</h3>

<p>Namespace injection is another variation on the IIFE where we "inject" the methods and properties for a specific namespace from within a function wrapper using <i>this</i> as a namespace proxy. The benefit this pattern offers is easy application of functional behaviour to multiple objects or namespaces and can come in useful when applying a set of base methods to be built on later (e.g. getters and setters).</p>
<p>The disadvantages of this pattern are that there may be easier or more optimal approaches to achieving this goal (e.g. deep object extension / merging) which I cover earlier in the section.</p>
<p>Below we can see an example of this pattern in action, where we use it to populate the behaviour for two namespaces: one initially defined (utils) and another which we dynamically create as a part of the functionality assignment for utils (a new namespace called <i>tools</i>).</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_74998"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>myApp = myApp || {};</code></div><div><code>myApp.utils = {};</code></div><div><code>(</code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>val = 5;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.getValue = </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>val;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.setValue = </code><code>function</code><code>( newVal ) {</code></div><div><code>val = newVal;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// also introduce a new sub-namespace</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.tools = {};</code></div><div><code>}).apply( myApp.utils );</code></div><div><code>// inject new behaviour into the tools namespace</code></div><div><code>// which we defined via the utilities module</code></div><div><code>(</code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.diagnose = </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>"diagnosis"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}).apply( myApp.utils.tools );</code></div><div><code>// note, this same approach to extension could be applied</code></div><div><code>// to a regular IIFE, by just passing in the context as</code></div><div><code>// an argument and modifying the context rather than just</code></div><div><code>// "this"</code></div><div><code>// Usage:</code></div><div><code>// Outputs our populated namespace</code></div><div><code>console.log( myApp );</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: 5</code></div><div><code>console.log( myApp.utils.getValue() );</code></div><div><code>// Sets the value of `val` and returns it</code></div><div><code>myApp.utils.setValue( 25 );</code></div><div><code>console.log( myApp.utils.getValue() );</code></div><div><code>// Testing another level down</code></div><div><code>console.log( myApp.utils.tools.diagnose() );</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>Angus Croll has also <a href="http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/scriptjunkie/gg578608" target="_blank">previously</a> suggested the idea of using the call API to provide a natural separation between contexts and arguments. This pattern can feel a lot more like a module creator, but as modules still offer an encapsulation solution, we'll briefly cover it for the sake of thoroughness:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_512039"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// define a namespace we can use later</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>ns = ns || {},</code></div><div><code>ns2 = ns2 || {};</code></div><div><code>// the module/namespace creator</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>creator = </code><code>function</code><code>( val ) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>val = val || 0;</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.next = </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>val++</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>this</code><code>.reset = </code><code>function</code> <code>() {</code></div><div><code>val = 0;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>creator.call( ns );</code></div><div><code>// ns.next, ns.reset now exist</code></div><div><code>creator.call( ns2, 5000 );</code></div><div><code>// ns2 contains the same methods</code></div><div><code>// but has an overridden value for val</code></div><div><code>// of 5000</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>As mentioned, this type of pattern is useful for assigning a similar base set of functionality to multiple modules or namespaces. I would however only really suggest using it where explicitly declaring functionality within an object/closure for direct access doesn't make sense.</p>

<h2>Advanced namespacing patterns</h2>

<p>We'll now explore some advanced patterns and utilities that I have found invaluable when working on larger applications - some of which have required a re-think of traditional approaches to application namespacing. I'll note that I am not advocating any of the following as <em>the</em> way to namespace, but rather ways that I have found work in practice.</p>

<h3>Automating nested namespacing</h3>
<p>As we've reviewed, nested namespaces can provide an organized hierarchy of structure for a unit of code. An example of such a namespace could be the following: <i>application.utilities.drawing.canvas.2d</i>. This can also be expanded using the object literal pattern to be:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_358569"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>var</code> <code>application = {</code></div><div><code>utilities: {</code></div><div><code>drawing: {</code></div><div><code>canvas: {</code></div><div><code>2d:{</code></div><div><code>//...</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>

<p>One of the obvious challenges with this pattern is that each additional layer we wish to create requires yet another object to be defined as a child of some parent in our top-level namespace. This can become particularly laborious when multiple depths are required as our application increases in complexity.</p>

<p>How can this problem be better solved? In <a href="http://www.amazon.com/JavaScript-Patterns-Stoyan-Stefanov/dp/0596806752" target="_blank">JavaScript Patterns</a>, <a href="http://jspatterns.com" target="_blank">Stoyan Stefanov</a> presents a very-clever approach for automatically defining nested namespaces under an existing global variable. He suggests a convenience method that takes a single string argument for a nest, parses this and automatically populates our base namespace with the objects required.</p>

<p>The method he suggests using is the following, which I've updated it to be a generic function for easier re-use with multiple namespaces:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_805344"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// top-level namespace being assigned an object literal</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>myApp = myApp || {};</code></div><div><code>// a convenience function for parsing string namespaces and</code></div><div><code>// automatically generating nested namespaces</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>extend( ns, ns_string ) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>parts = ns_string.split(</code><code>"."</code><code>),</code></div><div><code>parent = ns,</code></div><div><code>pl;</code></div><div><code>pl = parts.length;</code></div><div><code>for</code> <code>( </code><code>var</code> <code>i = 0; i &lt; pl; i++ ) {</code></div><div><code>// create a property if it doesn't exist</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( </code><code>typeof</code> <code>parent[parts[i]] === </code><code>"undefined"</code> <code>) {</code></div><div><code>parent[parts[i]] = {};</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>parent = parent[parts[i]];</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>parent;</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>// Usage:</code></div><div><code>// extend myApp with a deeply nested namespace</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>mod = extend(myApp, </code><code>"modules.module2"</code><code>);</code></div><div><code>// the correct object with nested depths is output</code></div><div><code>console.log(mod);</code></div><div><code>// minor test to check the instance of mod can also</code></div><div><code>// be used outside of the myApp namesapce as a clone</code></div><div><code>// that includes the extensions</code></div><div><code>// Outputs: true</code></div><div><code>console.log(mod == myApp.modules.module2);</code></div><div><code>// further demonstration of easier nested namespace</code></div><div><code>// assignment using extend</code></div><div><code>extend(myApp, </code><code>"moduleA.moduleB.moduleC.moduleD"</code><code>);</code></div><div><code>extend(myApp, </code><code>"longer.version.looks.like.this"</code><code>);</code></div><div><code>console.log(myApp);</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>Web inspector output:</p>
<p><div class="readableLargeImageContainer"><img src="images/ns1.png"  /></div></p>
<p>Where one would previously have had to explicitly declare the various nests for their namespace as objects, this can now be easily achieved using a single, cleaner line of code.</p>

<h3>Dependency declaration pattern</h3>
<p>We're now going to explore a minor augmentation to the nested namespacing pattern which we'll refer to as the Dependency Declaration pattern. We all know that local references to objects can decrease overall lookup times, but let's apply this to namespacing to see how it might look in practice:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_193166"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// common approach to accessing nested namespaces</code></div><div><code>myApp.utilities.math.fibonacci( 25 );</code></div><div><code>myApp.utilities.math.sin( 56 );</code></div><div><code>myApp.utilities.drawing.plot( 98,50,60 );</code></div><div><code>// with local/cached references</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>utils = myApp.utilities,</code></div><div><code>maths = utils.math,</code></div><div><code>drawing = utils.drawing;</code></div><div><code>// easier to access the namespace</code></div><div><code>maths.fibonacci( 25 );</code></div><div><code>maths.sin( 56 );</code></div><div><code>drawing.plot( 98, 50,60 );</code></div><div><code>// note that the above is particularly performant when</code></div><div><code>// compared to hundreds or thousands of calls to nested</code></div><div><code>// namespaces vs. a local reference to the namespace</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>Working with a local variable here is almost always faster than working with a top-level global (e.g.myApp). It's also both more convenient and more performant than accessing nested properties/sub-namespaces on every subsequent line and can improve readability in more complex applications.</p>

<p>Stoyan recommends declaring localized namespaces required by a function or module at the top of our function scope (using the single-variable pattern) and calls this a dependency declaration pattern. One of the benefits this offers is a decrease in locating dependencies and resolving them, should we have an extendable architecture that dynamically loads modules into our namespace when required.</p>

<p>In my opinion this pattern works best when working at a modular level, localizing a namespace to be used by a group of methods. Localizing namespaces on a per-function level, especially where there is significant overlap between namespace dependencies would be something I would recommend avoiding where possible. Instead, define it further up and just have them all access the same reference.</p>

<h3>Deep object extension</h3>
<p>An alternative approach to automatic namespacing is deep object extension. Namespaces defined using object literal notation may be easily extended (or merged) with other objects (or namespaces) such that the properties and functions of both namespaces can be accessible under the same namespace post-merge.</p>

<p>This is something that's been made fairly easy to accomplish with modern JavaScript frameworks (e.g. see jQuery's <a href="http://api.jquery.com/jQuery.extend/" target="_blank">$.extend</a>), however, if looking to extend object (namespaces) using vanilla JS, the following routine may be of assistance.</p>

<div><div id="highlighter_962266"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// extend.js</code></div><div><code>// Written by Andrew Dupont, optimized by Addy Osmani</code></div><div><code>function</code> <code>extend( destination, source ) {</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>toString = Object.prototype.toString,</code></div><div><code>objTest = toString.call({});</code></div><div><code>for</code> <code>( </code><code>var</code> <code>property </code><code>in</code> <code>source ) {</code></div><div><code>if</code> <code>( source[property] && objTest === toString.call(source[property]) ) {</code></div><div><code>destination[property] = destination[property] || {};</code></div><div><code>extend(destination[property], source[property]);</code></div><div><code>} </code><code>else</code> <code>{</code></div><div><code>destination[property] = source[property];</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>return</code> <code>destination;</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>console.group( </code><code>"objExtend namespacing tests"</code> <code>);</code></div><div><code>// define a top-level namespace for usage</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>myNS = myNS || {};</code></div><div><code>// 1. extend namespace with a "utils" object</code></div><div><code>extend(myNS, {</code></div><div><code>utils:{</code></div><div><code>//...</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"test 1"</code><code>, myNS);</code></div><div><code>// myNS.utils now exists</code></div><div><code>// 2. extend with multiple depths (namespace.hello.world.wave)</code></div><div><code>extend(myNS, {</code></div><div><code>hello: {</code></div><div><code>world: {</code></div><div><code>wave: {</code></div><div><code>test: </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>//...</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>// test direct assignment works as expected</code></div><div><code>myNS.hello.test1 = </code><code>"this is a test"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>myNS.hello.world.test2 = </code><code>"this is another test"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"test 2"</code><code>, myNS );</code></div><div><code>// 3. what if myNS already contains the namespace being added</code></div><div><code>// (e.g. "library")? we want to ensure no namespaces are being</code></div><div><code>// overwritten during extension</code></div><div><code>myNS.library = {</code></div><div><code>foo:</code><code>function</code> <code>() {}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>extend( myNS, {</code></div><div><code>library: {</code></div><div><code>bar: </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>//...</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>// confirmed that extend is operating safely (as expected)</code></div><div><code>// myNS now also contains library.foo, library.bar</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"test 3"</code><code>, myNS );</code></div><div><code>// 4. what if we wanted easier access to a specific namespace without having</code></div><div><code>// to type the whole namespace out each time?</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>shorterAccess1 = myNS.hello.world;</code></div><div><code>shorterAccess1.test3 = </code><code>"hello again"</code><code>;</code></div><div><code>console.log( </code><code>"test 4"</code><code>, myNS);</code></div><div><code>//success, myApp.hello.world.test3 is now "hello again"</code></div><div><code>console.groupEnd();</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p><strong>Note:</strong> The above implementation is not cross-browser compatible for all objects and should be considered a proof-of-concept only. One may find the Underscore.js <code>extend()</code> method a simpler, more cross-browser implementation to start with <a href="http://documentcloud.github.io/underscore/docs/underscore.html#section-82" target="_blank">http://documentcloud.github.io/underscore/docs/underscore.html#section-82</a>.</p>

<p>For developers that are going to use jQuery in their application, one can achieve the exact same object namespace extensibility with <code>$.extend</code> as follows:</p>
<div><div id="highlighter_570848"><table><tbody><tr><td></td><td><div><div><code>// top-level namespace</code></div><div><code>var</code> <code>myApp = myApp || {};</code></div><div><code>// directly assign a nested namespace</code></div><div><code>myApp.library = {</code></div><div><code>foo: </code><code>function</code><code>() {</code></div><div><code>//...</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>};</code></div><div><code>// deep extend/merge this namespace with another</code></div><div><code>// to make things interesting, let's say it's a namespace</code></div><div><code>// with the same name but with a different function</code></div><div><code>// signature: $.extend( deep, target, object1, object2 )</code></div><div><code>$.extend( </code><code>true</code><code>, myApp, {</code></div><div><code>library:{</code></div><div><code>bar: </code><code>function</code><code>(){</code></div><div><code>//...</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>}</code></div><div><code>});</code></div><div><code>console.log(</code><code>"test"</code><code>, myApp);</code></div><div><code>// myApp now contains both library.foo() and library.bar() methods</code></div><div><code>// nothing has been overwritten which is what we're hoping for.</code></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div>
<p>For the sake of thoroughness, please see <a href="https://gist.github.com/1221980" target="_blank">here</a> for jQuery $.extend equivalents to the rest of the namespacing experiments found in this section.</p>

<h3>Recommendation</h3>

<p>Reviewing the namespace patterns we've explored in this section, the option that I would personally use for most larger applications is nested object namespacing with the object literal pattern. Where possible, I would implement this using automated nested namespacing, however this is just a personal preference.</p>

<p>IIFEs and single global variables may work fine for applications in the small to medium range, however, larger codebases requiring both namespaces and deep sub-namespaces require a succinct solution that promotes readability and scales. I feel this pattern achieves all of these objectives well.</p>

<p>I would also recommend trying out some of the suggested advanced utility methods for namespace extension as they really can save us time in the long-run.</p>


<h1 id="conclusions">Conclusions</h1>
<p>That's it for this introductory adventure into the world of design patterns in JavaScript and jQuery - I hope you've found it beneficial.</p>

  <p>
  Design patterns make it easy for us to build on the shoulders of developers who have defined solutions to challenging problems and architectures over a number of decades. The contents of this book should hopefully provide sufficient information to get started using the patterns we covered in your own scripts, plugins and web applications.</p>

  <p>It's important for us to be aware of these patterns but it's also essential to know how and when to use them. Study the pros and cons of each pattern before employing them. Take the time out to experiment with patterns to fully appreciate what they offer and make usage judgements based on a pattern's true value to your application.</p>

<p>If I’ve encouraged your interest in this area further and you would like to learn more about design patterns, there are a number of excellent titles on this area available for generic software development and of course, JavaScript.</p>

<p>I am happy to recommend:</p>

<p>Thanks for reading <em>Learning JavaScript Design Patterns</em>. For more educational material on learning JavaScript, please feel free to read more from me on my blog at <a href="http://addyosmani.com" target="_blank">http://addyosmani.com</a> or on Twitter <a href="http://twitter.com/addyosmani" target="_blank">@addyosmani</a>.</p>
<p>Until next time, the very best of luck with your adventures in JavaScript!</p>
